The Critique of Reincarnation and the Attempt to Harmonize Wisdom and Revelation
Al-Jurjani criticizes reincarnation and cites al-Razi on the dual resurrection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Jurjani's criticism of the reincarnation theory as conjecture and Imam al-Razi's reference to those who affirm both spiritual and bodily resurrection.

Core Idea
Reincarnation is baseless conjecture; true resurrection involves both spiritual and bodily felicity.

This lesson presents the author's sharp critique of the entire reincarnationist framework, dismissing it as mere conjecture built on false premises of the pre-eternity and immateriality of souls, which he claims have already been refuted. It then introduces Imam al-Razi's mention of those who try to reconcile philosophy and sharia by affirming both spiritual and bodily resurrection, emphasizing that spiritual felicity lies in knowledge of God and bodily felicity in sensory perception, and that the combination is not possible in this life.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the shift from presenting the theory to criticizing it.
The phrase 'wa la yakhfa' indicates the author's evaluation.
2
Identify the author's criticism
Find the sentence where the author says this is all 'rajm bil-zann' (casting conjecture) and on what basis (pre-eternity and immateriality of souls).
The author says these two premises have already been refuted.
3
Understand al-Razi's mention
Read the quote from Imam al-Razi. He talks about those who affirm both spiritual and bodily resurrection and their argument.
The quote is incomplete but gives the gist of their position.
4
Consider the contrast
Think about why the author includes al-Razi's mention after his own critique. Is he endorsing it or just reporting?
Al-Razi's mention might be a positive alternative to the reincarnation theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذا ما عليه جمهورهم وقال قوم منهم ) أي من الفلاسفة (وهم أهل التناسخ : إنما تبقى مجردة) عن الأبدان ( النفوس الكاملة التي أخرجت قوتها إلى الفعل) ولم يبق شيء من الكمالات الممكنة لها بالقوة فصارت طاهرة عن جميع العلائق الجسمانية، وتخلصت إلى عالم القدس ، ( وأما ) النفوس ( الناقصة التي بقي شيء من كمالاتها بالقوة ) فإنها تتردد في الأبدان الإنسانية وتنتقل من بدن إلى بدن آخر حتى تبلغ النهاية فيما هو كمالها من علومها وأخلاقها، فحينئذ تبقى مجردة مطهرة عن التعلق بالأبدان ( ويسمى ) هذا الانتقال ( نسخاً وقيل: ربما تنازلت إلى ( الأبدان ( الحيوانية) فتنتقل من البدن الإنساني إلى بدن حيواني يناسبه في الأوصاف كبدن الأسد للشجاع والأرنب للجبان (ويسمى مسخاً، وقيل: ) ربما تنازلت (إلى) الأجسام ( النباتية ويسمى رسخاً وقيل : إلى الجمادية) كالمعادن والبسائط أيضاً. ويسمى فسخاً قالوا وهذه التنازلات المذكورة هي مراتب العقوبات وإليها الإشارة بما ورد من الدركات الضيقة في جهنم ( هذا في المتنازلة أو أما المتصاعدة ) من مرتبة إلى ما هو أكمل منها ( فقد تتخلص من الأبدان ) كلها ) لصيرورتها كاملة ) في جميع صفاتها، كما مر ، وقد تتعلق ببعض الأجرام السماوية لبقاء حاجتها إلى الاستكمال ولا يخفى أن ذلك كله رجم بالظن بناء على قدم النفوس وتجردها وقد أبطلناهما قال الإمام الرازي: وأما القائلون بالمعاد الروحاني والجسماني معاً فقد أرادوا أن يجمعوا بين الحكمة والشريعة، فقالوا : دل العقل على أن سعادة الأرواح بمعرفة الله تعالى ،ومحبته وأن سعادة الأجسام في إدراك المحسوسات والجمع بين هاتين السعادتين في هذه الحياة غير ممكن لأن الإنسان حتى لا يلحقها تكثر وتغير بوجه من الوجوه وكيف نسبة ترتب الموجودات إليها ثم كلما ازداد النظر استبصاراً ازداد للسعادة استعداداً .
This is the position of the majority of them. And a group of them – i.e., of the philosophers – who are the reincarnationists said: Only the perfect souls that have actualized their potential and have nothing left of possible perfections in potentiality remain disembodied from bodies, having become pure from all corporeal attachments and having reached the holy world. As for the imperfect souls that have some of their perfections remaining in potentiality, they wander among human bodies and transfer from one body to another until they reach the end in what is their perfection of sciences and character. Then they remain disembodied, purified from attachment to bodies. This transfer is called 'naskh'. It is said that sometimes they descend to animal bodies, transferring from a human body to an animal body that corresponds to them in qualities, like the body of a lion for the courageous and the rabbit for the cowardly, and it is called 'maskh'. It is said that sometimes they descend to plant bodies, and it is called 'raskh', and sometimes to mineral bodies like metals and simples, and it is called 'faskh'. They said: these aforementioned descents are levels of punishments, and they refer to what is reported about the narrow depths in Hell. This is regarding the descending ones. As for the ascending ones, from one level to what is more perfect, they may completely free themselves from bodies by becoming perfect in all attributes, as mentioned, and they may attach to some celestial bodies due to their remaining need for perfection. And it is not hidden that all of this is casting conjecture based on the pre-eternity of souls and their immateriality, and we have refuted these two [premises]. Imam al-Razi said: As for those who affirm both spiritual and bodily resurrection together, they intended to reconcile wisdom and religious law. They said: Reason indicates that the felicity of spirits lies in knowledge of God and love of Him, and the felicity of bodies lies in perceiving sensory things, and combining these two felicities in this life is impossible because man... so that multiplicity and change do not affect it in any way, and how the order of existents is related to it. Then the more one increases in insight through reflection, the more one increases in preparedness for felicity.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage presents the views of philosophers on the afterlife after summarizing the earlier discussion on soul states. It first states the position of the majority of philosophers (جمهورهم) – likely referring to the Peripatetic view that souls remain in their state. Then it introduces a dissenting group, the reincarnationists (أهل التناسخ). They believe that only perfect souls (those that have actualized all their potential) become disembodied and reach the holy world. Imperfect souls continue to transmigrate through human bodies until they become perfect. The passage then details the types of transmigration: نسخ (naskh) – transfer to another human body; مسخ (maskh) – descent to an animal body corresponding to one's qualities; رسخ (raskh) – descent to a plant body; فسخ (faskh) – descent to a mineral body. These are considered levels of punishment (مراتب العقوبات), alluded to in religious texts about the 'narrow depths' of Hell. For ascending souls (المتصاعدة) that become perfect, they either become completely free from bodies or attach to celestial bodies if they still need perfection. The author criticizes this entire theory as pure conjecture (رجم بالظن) based on two false premises: the pre-eternity of souls (قدم النفوس) and their immateriality (تجردها), which have already been refuted. Finally, it quotes Imam al-Razi who mentions those who affirm both spiritual and bodily resurrection, attempting to harmonize philosophy and sharia. They argue that reason shows spiritual felicity is in knowledge and love of God, while bodily felicity is in sensory perception, and the combination is impossible in this life. The quote from al-Razi is incomplete but emphasizes that increasing insight increases preparedness for felicity.

Key Terms
قدم النفوس
تجرد
معاد روحاني
معاد جسماني
رجم بالظن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think al-Razi is supporting reincarnation.
Correction: Al-Razi is presenting a different view that affirms both spiritual and bodily resurrection, not reincarnation.

Checkpoint
On what basis does the author claim the reincarnation theory is built?
A) The pre-eternity of souls and their immateriality
B) The belief in bodily resurrection
C) The authority of religious texts
D) The majority of philosophers

Key Takeaway
Al-Jurjani rejects reincarnation as conjecture, while al-Razi mentions a view that combines spiritual and bodily resurrection as a synthesis of philosophy and revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever harbored the thought that if you fail this life, your soul might get another chance in a new body? How does this passage expose that as baseless conjecture, and what does that mean for your urgency to repent today?
?
Al-Razi says spiritual felicity is in knowing and loving God, and bodily felicity is in sensory perception. Which one do you prioritize more in your daily choices—and are you neglecting the other? How will you balance them starting now?
?
The author states that the two premises of reincarnation have been refuted. Have you ever critically examined the foundations of your own beliefs about the afterlife, or have you accepted them without proof? What one step will you take this week to move from conjecture to certainty?

The Doctrine of Metempsychosis and Its Four Types
Philosophers' view of metempsychosis and its four levels

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' doctrine of metempsychosis, its four subdivisions (naskh, maskh, raskh, faskh), and why Islamic theologians reject it as conjecture.

Core Idea
Metempsychosis imagines souls cycling through bodies—human, animal, plant, mineral—until purified.

This passage presents a philosophical theory of transmigration, detailing how souls allegedly move through different levels of creation based on their perfection. The author then dismisses the entire framework as baseless conjecture built on false premises about the soul's pre-eternity and incorporeality, which have already been refuted.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to grasp the full theory of metempsychosis.
Pay attention to the distinction between perfect and imperfect souls.
2
Identify the soul types
Note how the passage divides souls into perfect and imperfect. Perfect souls are freed from bodies; imperfect souls transmigrate.
3
List the four types of transmigration
Identify the four Arabic terms: naskh (human to human), maskh (human to animal), raskh (human to plant), faskh (human to inanimate). Understand their meanings and examples.
These are considered levels of punishment for the descending souls.
4
Understand the refutation
Notice the concluding statement: the author dismisses all this as conjecture based on false premises (pre-eternity and incorporeality of souls) that were already refuted.
This shows the theological stance: the doctrine is rejected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال قوم منهم ) أي من الفلاسفة (وهم أهل التناسخ : إنما تبقى مجردة) عن الأبدان ( النفوس الكاملة التي أخرجت قوتها إلى الفعل) ولم يبق شيء من الكمالات الممكنة لها بالقوة فصارت طاهرة عن جميع العلائق الجسمانية، وتخلصت إلى عالم القدس ، ( وأما ) النفوس ( الناقصة التي بقي شيء من كمالاتها بالقوة ) فإنها تتردد في الأبدان الإنسانية وتنتقل من بدن إلى بدن آخر حتى تبلغ النهاية فيما هو كمالها من علومها وأخلاقها، فحينئذ تبقى مجردة مطهرة عن التعلق بالأبدان ( ويسمى ) هذا الانتقال ( نسخاً وقيل: ربما تنازلت إلى ( الأبدان ( الحيوانية) فتنتقل من البدن الإنساني إلى بدن حيواني يناسبه في الأوصاف كبدن الأسد للشجاع والأرنب للجبان (ويسمى مسخاً، وقيل: ) ربما تنازلت (إلى) الأجسام ( النباتية ويسمى رسخاً وقيل : إلى الجمادية) كالمعادن والبسائط أيضاً. ويسمى فسخاً قالوا وهذه التنازلات المذكورة هي مراتب العقوبات وإليها الإشارة بما ورد من الدركات الضيقة في جهنم ( هذا في المتنازلة أو أما المتصاعدة ) من مرتبة إلى ما هو أكمل منها ( فقد تتخلص من الأبدان ) كلها ) لصيرورتها كاملة ) في جميع صفاتها، كما مر ، وقد تتعلق ببعض الأجرام السماوية لبقاء حاجتها إلى الاستكمال ولا يخفى أن ذلك كله رجم بالظن بناء على قدم النفوس وتجردها وقد أبطلناهما
And a group among them, who are the proponents of metempsychosis, said: Only the perfect souls remain stripped of bodies, those who have actualized their potential and nothing of their possible perfections remains in potentiality, so they become pure from all physical attachments and attain to the world of sanctity. As for the imperfect souls in which something of their perfections remains in potentiality, they wander among human bodies and transfer from one body to another until they reach the end regarding what is their perfection in knowledge and morals; then they remain stripped and purified from attachment to bodies. This transfer is called naskh. It is said: Sometimes they descend to animal bodies, transferring from a human body to an animal body that matches them in attributes, like the body of a lion for the brave and the body of a rabbit for the cowardly. This is called maskh. It is said: Sometimes they descend to plant bodies, called raskh. And it is said to inanimate bodies, like minerals and simple substances, called faskh. They said: These descents mentioned are the levels of punishments, and to them refers what has been reported about the confined depths in Hell. This is regarding the descending ones. As for the ascending ones from a rank to what is more perfect, they may completely obtain release from bodies due to their becoming perfect in all their attributes, as previously mentioned, and they may attach to some celestial bodies due to the remaining need for perfection. And it is not hidden that all of this is conjecture based on the pre-eternity of souls and their incorporeality, which we have already refuted.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage presents the doctrine of metempsychosis (tanāsukh) among some philosophers. It distinguishes between perfect and imperfect souls. Perfect souls remain stripped. Imperfect souls transmigrate through human bodies until they perfect themselves. Then it enumerates four types of descent to lower bodies: naskh (human to human), maskh (human to animal), raskh (human to plant), and faskh (human to inanimate). These are considered punishments. Then it mentions ascending souls that may become free. Finally, the author refutes these ideas as baseless conjecture (rajm bi-l-ẓann) based on the false premises of pre-eternity and incorporeality of souls, which have already been refuted.

Key Terms
تناسخ
نسخ
مسخ
رسخ
فسخ
نفوس كاملة
نفوس ناقصة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The four categories of metempsychosis are sometimes confused with Islamic concepts of punishment in the afterlife.
Correction: The passage shows that these are part of a philosophical system that the author rejects as conjecture, not Islamic doctrine.

Checkpoint
According to the doctrine of tanāsukh, what happens to a cowardly person's soul after death?
A) It becomes attached to a celestial body.
B) It is reborn as a rabbit.
C) It is annihilated.
D) It remains in a state of bliss.

Key Takeaway
The doctrine of metempsychosis is a philosophical theory of soul transmigration that Islamic theologians reject as speculative and unfounded.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you sometimes live as if you have endless chances to purify your soul, delaying repentance? How does the Islamic teaching that this life is your only opportunity change that mindset?
?
The philosophers thought souls perfect themselves over many lives. In what area of your life are you relying on gradual 'self-improvement' without a decisive turn to Allah?
?
This doctrine is dismissed as conjecture. Are there beliefs you hold about the unseen that are based on speculation rather than the Quran and Sunnah? What step will you take today to replace guesswork with certainty?

The Absolute Deniers of Resurrection
Reports the view of those who deny all resurrection, claiming the soul is a mixture.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the position of those who deny resurrection absolutely, including their definition of the soul and its implication for afterlife.

Core Idea
If the soul is a mixture, it dies with the body; re-creation of nothing is impossible.

Among the various views on resurrection, some philosophers denied it entirely, arguing that the soul is nothing more than a temporary blend of bodily elements. This lesson introduces their core claim and the logical consequence that makes resurrection impossible in their framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic sentence and its translation carefully. Focus on the key claim: 'al-nafs hiya al-mizāj'.
2
Identify the group and their premise
Note that the passage explicitly names 'those who deny resurrection absolutely' and states their definition of the soul as the temperament.
3
Understand the consequence
If the soul is identical with the temperament, then when the body dies, the temperament dissolves and the soul ceases to exist. Resurrection would require re-creating what no longer exists, which they deem impossible.
This shows why they deny both bodily and spiritual resurrection.
4
Connect to the broader topic
Consider how this position contrasts with other views that affirm resurrection. It represents the most extreme denial in the discussion of sam'iyyāt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المنكرون للمعاد مطلقاً هي فهم الذين قالوا : النفس هي المزاج فإذا مات الإنسان فقد عدمت النفس وإعادة المعدوم عندهم محال
As for those who deny resurrection absolutely, they are the ones who said: The soul is the temperament. So when a person dies, the soul becomes non-existent. And the re-creation of the non-existent is impossible according to them.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This short passage identifies a group that denies any form of resurrection. They define the soul as a mixture (mizāj) of bodily elements. Upon death, the mixture dissolves and the soul ceases to exist. Since they consider re-creation of the non-existent impossible, they deny both spiritual and bodily resurrection. This represents the most extreme denial among the philosophical positions discussed.

Key Terms
النفس هي المزاج
إعادة المعدوم
إنكار المعاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This group is often confused with materialists who deny survival after death without a specific argument.
Correction: Their argument is based on defining the soul as a temperament, which dissolves at death, making re-creation logically impossible. They are not simply materialists but have a specific metaphysical claim about the nature of the soul.

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the absolute deniers' rejection of resurrection?
A) The soul is immortal.
B) The soul is a temperament that perishes with death.
C) God does not have power to resurrect.
D) The resurrection is metaphorical.

Key Takeaway
The absolute deniers of resurrection define the soul as a transient mixture (mizāj) that ends at death, making resurrection impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that your soul is merely a temporary mixture that ends at death, how would your priorities change? Now, as a believer in resurrection, are you living with that same urgency?
?
When you face trials, do you ever feel like this life is all there is? How does the false argument of the deniers (that resurrection is impossible) tempt you to despair? What specific truth from this lesson must you cling to?
?
Are you actively preparing for the resurrection you claim to believe in? If someone asked you why resurrection is possible, could you explain the flaw in the deniers' logic? What is at stake if you are not ready?

The First Proof: Threat Requires Actual Punishment
First proof of the Mu'tazila for obligatory punishment and its Ash'ari answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's first proof for obligatory punishment and why the Ash'aris distinguish between occurrence and obligation.

Core Idea
A threat's fulfillment does not require its obligation.

The Mu'tazila and Khawarij argued that God's explicit threats of punishment for major sins must be carried out, otherwise His word would be broken. The Ash'ari response reframes the argument, showing that the threat can still be fulfilled even if punishment is not obligatory. Let's see how the first proof and its answer unfold.

Guided Path
1
Read the first proof
Read the passage where the Mu'tazila argue that if God does not punish, it entails khulf (breach) and kadhib (lying).
Notice they equate lack of punishment with forgiveness.
2
Examine the Ash'ari answer
The answer says 'its outcome is the occurrence of punishment—so where is its obligation?' Understand why this is a grammatical shift.
The answer denies that absence of obligation implies absence of occurrence.
3
Consider the counter-objection
The text includes a potential objection: if non-obligation allows possibility of breach, that is also impossible. Read the response denying that impossibility.
The response asserts that such actions are within God's power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما العقاب ففيه بحثان . الأول : أوجب جميع المعتزلة والخوارج عقاب صاحب الكبيرة ) إذا مات بلا توبة ولم يجوزوا أن يعفو الله عنه ( لوجهين الأول : أنه ( تعالى ( أوعد بالعقاب ( على الكبائر ( وأخبر به ) أي بالعقاب عليها ( فلو لم يعاقب ) على الكبيرة وعفا ) لزم الخلف في وعيده والكذب في خبره وإنه محال . الجواب : غايته وقوع العقاب فأين وجوبه ) الذي كلامنا فيه إذ لا شبهة في أن عدم الوجوب مع الوقوع لا يستلزم خلفاً ولا كذباً، لا يقال: إنه يستلزم جوازهما وهو أيضاً محال لأنا نقول : استحالته ممنوعة كيف وهما من الممكنات التي تشملها قدرته تعالى (الثاني: أنه إذا علم المذنب ) أي المرتكب للكبيرة ( أنه لا يعاقب على ذنبه ) بل يعفى عنه لم ينزجر عن الذنب بل ( كان ذلك تقريراً له على ذنبه ) وعدم التوبة عنه ( و ) كان ( إغراء للغير عليه وإنه قبيح مناف لمقصود الدعوة ) إلى الطاعات وترك المنهيات ( الجواب : منع تضمنه ) أي تضمن عدم وجود العقاب ( للتقرير والإغراء، إذ شمول الوعيد وتعريض الكل للعقاب وظن الوفاء بالوعيد فيه من الزجر والردع ما لا يخفى، واحتمال العفو عن البعض احتمالاً مرجواً لا ينافي ذلك)، يعني أن الوعيد عام يتناول كل واحد من المذنبين بظاهره الذي يقتضي ظن الوفاء به في حقه فيحصل لكل منهم الظن بكونه معاقبا بذنبه، وذلك كاف في زجر العاقل عن استقراره على ذنبه بعدم التوبة عنه وفي ردع غيره عن اقترافه وأما توهم العفو الناشئ من عدم وجود العقاب فاحتمال مرجوح لا يعارض ظن العقاب المقتضي للانزجار فقد ظهر أن المذنب لا علم له بأنه لا يعاقب بل ولا يظن ذلك ظناً فلا تقرير ولا إغراء . قوله : ( والجواب منه وجوب الغرض ولو سلم فالغرض التفضل بالنفع فأين الوجوب . قوله : ( لأنا نقول : استحالته ممنوعة) فإن قلت : الكذب نقص يستحيل عليه تعالى إجماعاً، ولا شك أن جواز المحال محال . قلت : الظاهر أن هذا الكلام بالنسبة إلى المعتزلة وهم لا يقولون إلا بالكلام اللفظي، وقد سبق أن النقص في الكلام اللفظي من قبيل القبح العقلي الذي نحن لا نقول به نعم ثبت بخبر النبي عليه السلام انتفاء الكذب في كلامه مطلقاً، وأما أنه أمر محال في نفسه بناء على أنه نقص فممنوع بقي هاهنا بحث وهو أن مراد المعتزلة بكون الشيء واجباً عليه تعالى أن الحالة اللائقة والحكمة المناسبة لمثل ذلك الحكيم أن يأتي به لا أنه بحيث لا يكون مقدوراً له وإلا لكان الباري تعالى موجباً بالنسبة إليه وهم مع إيجابهم عليه تعالى ما أوجبوه قائلون بكون الله تعالى مختاراً بلا خلاف منهم فعلى هذا اندفاع أصل استدلالهم بما ذكروه في حيز الجواب محل كلام فليتأمل. ممتنع عقلاً
And as for punishment, it has two researches. The first: All the Mu'tazila and Khawarij made obligatory the punishment of the committer of a major sin if he dies without repentance, and they did not allow that God would forgive him, for two reasons. The first: that He, exalted, threatened with punishment for major sins and informed of it (i.e., of punishment upon them). So if He did not punish (the major sin) and forgave, there would be a breach of promise in His threat and lying in His report, and that is impossible. The answer: its outcome is the occurrence of punishment—so where is its obligation, which is our topic? For there is no doubt that the absence of obligation with occurrence does not entail breach nor lying. Let it not be said that it entails their permissibility, and that is also impossible, for we say: its impossibility is denied—how? While they are from among the possibilities that His power encompasses. The second: that if the sinner, i.e., the committer of the major sin, knows that he will not be punished for his sin but will be forgiven, he will not desist from the sin; rather, that would be a confirmation for him in his sin and his not repenting from it, and it would be an incitement for others to it, and that is ugly and contrary to the purpose of the call to obedience and leaving prohibitions. The answer: denial that it (i.e., the absence of punishment) entails confirmation and incitement, for the comprehensiveness of the threat and the exposure of all to punishment and the expectation of fulfillment of the threat contains deterrence and prevention that is not hidden, and the possibility of forgiveness for some, being a hoped-for probability, does not negate that. This means that the threat is general and includes every one of the sinners by its apparent meaning which necessitates the expectation of its fulfillment in his case, so each of them obtains the expectation of being punished for his sin, and that suffices to deter the rational person from persisting in his sin by not repenting and to deter others from committing it. And as for the illusion of forgiveness arising from the absence of punishment, it is a weak probability that does not oppose the expectation of punishment that necessitates deterrence. So it has become clear that the sinner has no knowledge that he will not be punished, nor does he even expect that as a strong probability, so there is no confirmation or incitement. His saying: (and the answer from it is the obligation of purpose—even if conceded, the purpose is the favor of benefit—so where is the obligation?) His saying: (for we say: its impossibility is denied) for if you say: lying is a deficiency that is impossible for Him, exalted, by consensus, and there is no doubt that the permissibility of the impossible is impossible. I say: It is apparent that this statement is in relation to the Mu'tazila, and they do not affirm except verbal speech, and it has preceded that deficiency in verbal speech is of the type of rational ugliness which we do not affirm. Yes, it is established by the report of the Prophet, peace be upon him, that lying is negated from His speech absolutely. As for it being impossible in itself based on its being a deficiency, it is denied. There remains here a research: that the meaning of the Mu'tazila of something being obligatory upon Him, exalted, is that the suitable state and appropriate wisdom for such a wise one is that He should bring it about, not that it would not be within His power—otherwise the Creator would be constrained in relation to it. And they, despite making obligatory upon Him what they obligated, hold that God is free and choice (mukhtar) without dispute from them. So on this basis, the rebuttal of the foundation of their argument by what they mentioned in the context of the answer is a matter for reflection. It is rationally impossible.
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage is the first research (al-baḥth al-awwal) within the discussion of punishment. It presents the position of the Mu'tazila and Khawarij, who assert that punishment for a major sin is obligatory upon God if the sinner dies without repentance. They give two proofs. The first proof: God has explicitly threatened punishment for major sins; if He were to forgive instead of punishing, that would entail a breach of promise (khulf) and lying (kadhib), both impossible for God. The answer from the Ash'ari perspective is that the argument only establishes that punishment may occur, not that it is obligatory; lack of obligation does not entail breach or lying, because the threat could still be fulfilled even if not obligatory. A possible counter-objection that allowing permissibility contradicts God's perfection is dismissed by stating that such actions are possible and within His power. The second proof: if sinners knew they would not be punished, they would not be deterred, leading to confirmation in sin and incitement for others, which is evil. The answer distinguishes between certainty and probability: the general threat creates a strong expectation of punishment in each sinner's mind, which suffices for deterrence. The possibility of forgiveness is a weak probability that does not undermine this expectation. The passage then includes a gloss (qāla) that delves into the first answer, discussing the Mu'tazila's notion of obligation: for them, it means what is suitable for the wise, not a constraint on divine power. The gloss argues that even on their terms, the argument might not stand. The moral of the research is that punishment is not obligatory; God may forgive or punish as He wills, and His threats are still effective for deterrence.

Key Terms
وعيد
خلف
كذب
وجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's threat must be carried out exactly as stated or He would be a liar.
Correction: God's threat is general and can be fulfilled in some cases; not fulfilling it in every individual case does not make Him a liar, especially if the threat is conditional or the punishment is not obligatory.

Checkpoint
What does the Ash'ari answer say about the Mu'tazila's first proof?
A) It proves obligation
B) It only shows occurrence, not obligation
C) It shows that forgiveness is impossible
D) It proves that breach is impossible

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's first proof confuses the occurrence of punishment with its obligation; the Ash'aris show that a threat can be fulfilled without being obligatory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's threats are real but not automatic, does that make you more or less careful about your sins? Will you repent today with full sincerity, knowing that your safety is not guaranteed by obligation but by His mercy?
?
The Mu'tazila view forces accountability by making punishment necessary. The Ash'ari view leaves it to God's will. Which perspective stirs you to take your major sins more seriously? What change in your daily actions would reflect that seriousness?
?
When you read a verse of threat (like about major sins), do you feel fear of automatic punishment, or do you trust that God could still forgive? How does your answer shape your relationship with Allah—are you driven by fear or hope? Commit to one concrete act of obedience today in response.

The Second Proof: Deterrence Requires Certain Punishment
Second proof of the Mu'tazila for obligatory punishment and its Ash'ari answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's second proof based on deterrence and the Ash'ari response that general threat suffices through probability.

Core Idea
A general threat deters even without certain punishment for every sinner.

The Mu'tazila argued that if sinners knew they would not be punished, they would persist in sin and others would imitate them, making the divine call ineffective. The Ash'ari response cleverly uses the concept of probability: the general threat creates a strong expectation of punishment in each sinner's mind, which is enough to deter rational people. Let's examine this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof
Read the Mu'tazila claim that knowledge of non-punishment leads to confirmation in sin and incitement.
Note their premise that knowledge of forgiveness would remove deterrence.
2
Study the Ash'ari answer
The answer denies that absence of punishment entails such knowledge. The threat is general and creates a strong expectation (zann) of punishment in each individual.
The key is that the sinner does not know he will be forgiven; he only has a weak hope.
3
Understand the role of probability
The text says the general threat suffices for deterrence because each sinner expects punishment for himself. The possibility of forgiveness is a weak probability that does not undermine the deterrent.
This shows how divine threats work even if not all are punished.

Source Text

The Meaning of Obligation According to the Mu'tazila
Gloss on the Mu'tazila's concept of obligation as suitability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's concept of divine obligation as suitability (liyāqa) rather than constraint, and how this affects the debate.

Core Idea
For the Mu'tazila, obligation on God means it is suitable for His wisdom, not that it is necessary.

The Mu'tazila's claim that punishment is obligatory upon God might be misunderstood. In this gloss, the author explains that for the Mu'tazila, 'obligatory' means what is fitting for a wise being to do, not that God is constrained. This nuance affects the strength of their argument. Let's explore this clarification.

Guided Path
1
Read the gloss
Read the final paragraph about the Mu'tazila's definition of obligation. Note the distinction between 'suitable' and 'necessary'.
The author says the Mu'tazila do not mean that God cannot do otherwise.
2
Understand the implication
If obligation is only suitability, then the Ash'ari answer that 'where is the obligation' might need re-examination. The text says 'on this basis, the rebuttal... is a matter for reflection.'
This suggests that the Ash'ari answer might not fully refute the Mu'tazila on their own terms.
3
Reflect on the debate
Consider how defining terms affects theological arguments. The Ash'ari and Mu'tazila have different concepts of obligation, leading to different conclusions.

His saying: (for we say: its impossibility is denied) for if you say: lying is a deficiency that is impossible for Him, exalted, by consensus, and there is no doubt that the permissibility of the impossible is impossible. I say: It is apparent that this statement is in relation to the Mu'tazila, and they do not affirm except verbal speech, and it has preceded that deficiency in verbal speech is of the type of rational ugliness which we do not affirm. Yes, it is established by the report of the Prophet, peace be upon him, that lying is negated from His speech absolutely. As for it being impossible in itself based on its being a deficiency, it is denied. There remains here a research: that the meaning of the Mu'tazila of something being obligatory upon Him, exalted, is that the suitable state and appropriate wisdom for such a wise one is that He should bring it about, not that it would not be within His power—otherwise the Creator would be constrained in relation to it. And they, despite making obligatory upon Him what they obligated, hold that God is free and choice (mukhtar) without dispute from them. So on this basis, the rebuttal of the foundation of their argument by what they mentioned in the context of the answer is a matter for reflection. It is rationally impossible.
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage is the first research (al-baḥth al-awwal) within the discussion of punishment. It presents the position of the Mu'tazila and Khawarij, who assert that punishment for a major sin is obligatory upon God if the sinner dies without repentance. They give two proofs. The first proof: God has explicitly threatened punishment for major sins; if He were to forgive instead of punishing, that would entail a breach of promise (khulf) and lying (kadhib), both impossible for God. The answer from the Ash'ari perspective is that the argument only establishes that punishment may occur, not that it is obligatory; lack of obligation does not entail breach or lying, because the threat could still be fulfilled even if not obligatory. A possible counter-objection that allowing permissibility contradicts God's perfection is dismissed by stating that such actions are possible and within His power. The second proof: if sinners knew they would not be punished, they would not be deterred, leading to confirmation in sin and incitement for others, which is evil. The answer distinguishes between certainty and probability: the general threat creates a strong expectation of punishment in each sinner's mind, which suffices for deterrence. The possibility of forgiveness is a weak probability that does not undermine this expectation. The passage then includes a gloss (qāla) that delves into the first answer, discussing the Mu'tazila's notion of obligation: for them, it means what is suitable for the wise, not a constraint on divine power. The gloss argues that even on their terms, the argument might not stand. The moral of the research is that punishment is not obligatory; God may forgive or punish as He wills, and His threats are still effective for deterrence.

Key Terms
وجوب الغرض
لائقة
حكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila think God is forced to punish.
Correction: They think it is suitable and wise for God to punish, not that He is compelled.

Checkpoint
According to the gloss, what do the Mu'tazila mean when they say something is obligatory upon God?
A) It is necessary and cannot be otherwise
B) It is suitable and wise for God to do
C) It is impossible for God to do
D) It is forbidden

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila view obligation on God as what is fitting for His wisdom, not as constraint, which nuances the debate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed someone's position because you assumed their words meant exactly what you think they mean? This passage shows that even 'obligatory' can be misunderstood. Where in your life do you need to stop and ask, 'What do they really mean by that?' before judging?
?
When you think about God's actions, do you secretly believe He is 'obliged' to act in a certain way because of your own sense of justice? This lesson teaches that even the Mu'tazila, who emphasize God's wisdom, still affirm His freedom. Is your heart truly at rest with God's absolute freedom, even when His ways do not suit your sense of what is 'fitting'?
?
The Ash'ari response in the passage says 'It is rationally impossible.' Do you sometimes rely on human reason as the ultimate judge of what God can or cannot do? This lesson challenges that assumption. Will you submit your reason to revelation even when it seems 'impossible' to you?

Refuting the Mu'tazila Claim of Eternal Punishment
Al-Sanusi presents the Mu'tazila argument and the Ash'ari refutation with glosses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila rational argument for the eternal punishment of the major sinner and the Ash'ari refutation based on the denial of desert and the possibility of cancellation.

Core Idea
No creature can place a demand on God; eternal punishment is not a necessary right.

This lesson examines the Mu'tazila claim that a major sinner who dies without repentance will be eternally punished. Their proof depends on the idea that punishment is pure harm and reward is pure benefit, which cannot coexist. The Ash'ari response deconstructs this by denying that any human inherently deserves reward or punishment, and by showing that even if they did, cancellation is possible.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila argument
Read the first section describing the Mu'tazila and Khawarij position and their rational proof. Note the key concepts: pure harm, pure benefit, and the impossibility of combining them.
The argument is that the sinner deserves punishment, which is pure and everlasting, and that cancels any reward.
2
Identify the first part of the answer
The Ash'ari response begins with 'man' al-istiḥqāq' (denial of desert). Understand why no one deserves anything from God.
This is based on the principle that no one has a claim on God.
3
Understand the second part: denial of perpetuity
The answer also denies that punishment must be everlasting (man' qayd al-dawām). Read the response to the objection about purity and perpetuity.
4
Learn the third response: possibility of cancellation
Even if we accept the attributes, the two deserts can cancel each other out (tasāqutān), and the sinner may enter Paradise by favor.
This is based on the principle of muḥābaṭa.
5
Review the glosses
The final part contains glosses clarifying the meaning of 'deserved punishment' and addressing circularity concerns. Reflect on these nuances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث الثاني : قالت المعتزلة والخوارج : صاحب الكبيرة إذا لم يتب عنها مخلد في النار ولا يخرج عنها أبداً، وعمدتهم في إثبات ما ادعوه دليل عقلي هو ( أن الفاسق يستحق العقاب) بفسقه واستحقاق العقاب بل العقاب (مضرة خالصة لا يشوبها ما يخالفها (دائمة) لا تنقطع أبداً، ( واستحقاق الثواب) بل الثواب (منفعة خالصة ) عن الشوائب ( دائمة والجمع بينهما ) أي بين استحقاقهما ( محال)، كما أن الجمع بينهما محال فإذا ثبت للفاسق استحقاق العقاب وجب أن يزول عنه استحقاق الثواب فيكون عذابه مخلداً . ( الجواب : منع الاستحقاق ) فإن المطيع لا يستحق بطاعته ثوابا والعاصي لا يستحق بمعصيته عقاباً، إذ قد ثبت أنه لا لأحد على الله وقد أجبنا عن دليل وجوب العقاب آنفاً، ( ومنع قيد الدوام) لا يقال : إذا كانت المضرة أو المنفعة منقطعة لم تكن خالصة لأنا نقول : ذلك ممنوع لجواز أن يخلق الله تعالى في المثاب والمعاقب العلم بذلك الانقطاع فلا يحصل للأول حزن ولا للثاني فرح على أن قيد الخلوص مما يتطرق إليه المنع أيضاً وما يتمسك به أنه لا بد من انفصالها عن مضار الدنيا ومنافعها، ولا تنفصل إلا بالخلوص ضعيف ( ثم) إنا بعد تسليمنا لما ذكرتم من صفات الثواب والعقاب نقول: (إنه قد يتساقطان ) فإن كلامكم مبني على المحابطة، وحينئذ جاز أن يتساقط الاستحقاقان معاً ويدخل ) صاحب الكبيرة ( الجنة تفضلاً كما قال تعالى) حكاية عن أهل الجنة : يجب من قوله : ( واستحقاق العقاب) أي العقاب المستحق كما في قولهم: حصول الصورة فلا اتجاه لقوله : بل العقاب إن لم يرد به بیان حاصل المعنى كما يشعر به لفظة بل. قوله : ( لجواز أن لا يخلق الله تعالى إلخ) فإن قلت لا فائدة لذلك لانقطاع حينئذ وأنه كعدمه، وجواز كونه مشتملاً على فائدة وحكمة لا نعرفها مما لا شك في بعده قلت : ليس المراد عدم خلق العلم بالانقطاع بعد وقوعه حتى يرد ما ذكرتم، بل المراد عدم خلق العلم بانقطاع العقاب مثلاً حال كونه معاقباً حتى ينتفع بملاحظة هذا الانقطاع وينافي الخلوص قوله: (وما يتمسك به إلخ) حاصل التمسك أن الثواب والعقاب لا يتميزان عن مضار الدنيا ومنافعها إلا بأن الدنيوية غير خالصة عن الضد فيكونان خالصين ووجه الضعف ظاهر لجواز أن يكون كل غير خالص عن المخالف مع الامتياز بوجوه أخر فضلاً عن الوجه الواحد قوله : ( فإن كلامهم مبني على المحابطة) فيه بحث إذ سيصرح في المقصد السابع أن قصة الإحباط مبنية على استحقاق العقاب ومنافاته للثواب، فبناؤه عليه دور، اللهم إلا أن يقال : معنى البناء على المحابطة أنه لا يتم إلا بالقول بها أولاً وآخراً، فليتأمل.
The second research: The Mu'tazila and Khawarij said: The committer of a major sin, if he does not repent from it, is eternally in the Fire and will never leave it. Their mainstay in proving what they claim is an intellectual proof: that the sinner deserves punishment for his sin, and deserving punishment—rather, punishment—is pure harm untainted by anything contrary, everlasting, never ceasing, and deserving reward—rather, reward—is pure benefit free of defects, everlasting; and combining the two (i.e., the two deserts) is impossible. Thus if punishment-desert is established for the sinner, reward-desert must cease from him, so his torment is eternal. The answer: Denial of desert—for the obedient does not deserve reward for his obedience, nor does the disobedient deserve punishment for his disobedience, since it has been established that no one has any claim upon God, and we have already answered the proof for obligatory punishment. And denial of the condition of perpetuity. Let it not be said: if the harm or benefit is intermittent, it is not pure. For we say: that is denied because it is possible that God, exalted, creates in the rewarded and the punished knowledge of that interruption, so that the former does not get grief nor the latter joy. Moreover, the condition of purity is also open to denial. And what they cling to—that they must be distinguished from worldly harms and benefits and cannot be distinguished except by purity—is weak. Then, after conceding to you the attributes of reward and punishment as mentioned, we say: it is possible that they cancel each other, for your speech is built on the principle of mutual cancellation (muḥābaṭa). In that case, it is possible that the two deserts cancel together, and the committer of the major sin enters Paradise as a favor, as God Almighty said, relating the words of the people of Paradise: ... His saying: (and deserving punishment) i.e., the deserved punishment, as in their saying: the attainment of the form, so there is no direction to his saying: 'rather, punishment' if he did not intend by it to clarify the meaning as indicated by the word 'bal'. His saying: (concerning the possibility that God does not create) etc. For if you say: There is no benefit in that because then it is intermittent and like non-existence, and the possibility that it includes benefit and wisdom we do not know is undoubtedly unlikely. I say: The intended meaning is not the absence of creating knowledge of the interruption after its occurrence so that what you mentioned would apply; rather, the intended meaning is the absence of creating knowledge of the interruption of punishment, for example, while he is being punished, so that he may benefit from observing this interruption, and it is contrary to purity. His saying: (and what they cling to, etc.) The summary of their clinging is that reward and punishment are not distinguished from worldly harms and benefits except by the fact that worldly ones are not free from their opposite, so they (reward and punishment) are pure. The weakness is apparent, because it is possible that each is not free from the opposite while being distinguished by other aspects, let alone one aspect. His saying: (for their speech is built on mutual cancellation) contains a discussion, for he will state explicitly in the seventh goal that the issue of mutual cancellation is built on the desert of punishment and its opposition to reward; so building it on it is a circle—unless it is said: the meaning of 'built on mutual cancellation' is that it is not complete except by affirming it first and last; so let it be reflected upon.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This is the second research (al-baḥth al-thānī), dealing with the Mu'tazila and Khawarij doctrine that a person who dies on a major sin without repentance will be eternally punished in Hellfire and never leave it. Their central proof is rational: the sinner deserves punishment, which is pure and everlasting harm; and if he also had any reward-desert from previous obedience, that reward would be pure and everlasting benefit. Since combining both pure and opposite deserts in one person is impossible, the punishment-desert must cancel the reward-desert, resulting in eternal torment. The Ash'ari answer is twofold. First, they deny the very notion of 'desert' (istiḥqāq): no creature has a right over God that demands reward or punishment; it is purely God's favor or justice. Second, they deny that the punishment must be everlasting; it could be temporary. A possible objection—that only what is pure can be distinguished from worldly harms—is dismissed because God could create knowledge of the interruption, making even temporary punishment or reward effective. Moreover, purity is not necessary for distinction. Then the text offers a further argument: even if we accept your description of reward and punishment, they could cancel each other out (taṣāqutān) through the principle of mutual cancellation (muḥābaṭa). If both deserts are equal, they could cancel, and the sinner could enter Paradise by mere favor (tafaḍḍul). A gloss (qāla) clarifies that the phrase 'deserved punishment' means the punishment that is deserved, not the accident. Another gloss discusses a possible objection about the benefit of knowing the interruption, explaining that the intention is not to remove knowledge of interruption after it occurs, but to prevent knowledge of the interruption during the punishment itself so that the punishment remains pure. A final gloss raises a circularity: the mutakallimūn base the cancellation on desert, but desert itself is in question; however, the context may soften this. Overall, the lesson refutes the notion of eternal punishment by denying desert and the necessity of perpetuity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The major sinner necessarily deserves eternal punishment because punishment is pure harm and cannot combine with pure benefit.
Correction: Ash'aris hold that no creature has a right to demand reward or punishment; it is all by God's will. Also, even if desert were accepted, punishment need not be everlasting.

Checkpoint
What is the central Ash'ari response to the Mu'tazila proof for eternal punishment?
A) Denial of desert
B) Denial of punishment altogether
C) Affirmation of eternal reward
D) Claim that major sin is not punished

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari refutation of the Mu'tazila argument for eternal punishment rests on denying any creature's right to demand reward or punishment and showing that even if such rights existed, they could cancel out.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
When you sin, do you secretly assume you must face eternal consequences because you 'deserve' them? How does this lesson challenge that assumption and reshape your hope in God's mercy?
?
The Mu'tazila argument reduces reward and punishment to rigid categories of pure benefit and pure harm. In your life, do you sometimes view God's dealings with you as purely punitive or purely rewarding, forgetting that He may have purposes beyond your understanding? How can you train yourself to submit to His wisdom even when His actions seem mixed?

The Murji'a and Mu'tazila Views on Nullification
Contrasts Murji'a and Mu'tazila positions on nullification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the contrasting Murji'a and Mu'tazila positions on nullification (al-ihbat): the Murji'a say faith nullifies all sins, while the Mu'tazila say a single major sin nullifies all reward.

Core Idea
Two extremes: faith erases all sin vs. one sin erases all good.

Before exploring the more complex weighting theories of al-Jubba'i and his son, we must first grasp two opposing positions: the Murji'a and the Mu'tazila. These represent the two poles in the debate on how sin and faith interact. This passage sets the stage by contrasting the Murji'a doctrine that faith nullifies all lapses with the Mu'tazila doctrine that one major sin nullifies all acts of obedience.

Guided Path
1
Read the Murji'a position
Read the first part of the passage: 'وذهبت المرجئة إلى أن الإيمان يحبط الزلات فلا عقاب على زلة مع الإيمان كما لا ثواب لطاعة مع الكفر'. Notice that they equate the effect of faith on sin with the effect of disbelief on good deeds.
The Murji'a see faith as a protective shield.
2
Read the Mu'tazila position
Read the second part: 'وقالت المعتزلة إن كبيرة واحدة تحبط ثواب جميع الطاعات وإن زادت على زلته'. They hold that even one major sin overrides all good deeds.
This is the opposite extreme.
3
Contrast the two
Notice the symmetry: for Murji'a, faith negates sin; for Mu'tazila, sin negates good deeds. Which side is prioritized?
This contrast sets up the need for a balanced theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهبت المرجئة إلى أن الإيمان يحبط الزلات فلا عقاب على زلة مع الإيمان كما لا ثواب لطاعة مع الكفر، وقالت المعتزلة إن كبيرة واحدة تحبط ثواب جميع الطاعات وإن زادت على زلته وذهب الجبائي وابنه إلى رعاية الكثرة في المحبط وزعما أن من زادت طاعاته على زلاته أحبطت عقاب زلاته وكفرتها، ومن زادت زلاته على طاعاته أحبطت ثواب طاعاته، ثم اختلفا فقال الجبائي: إذا زادت الطاعات أحبطت الزلات بأسرها من غير أن ينقص من ثواب الطاعات شيء، وإذا زادت أحبطت الطاعات برمتها من غير أن ينقص من عقاب الزلات شيء، وقال الإمام الرازي: مذهب الجبائي أن الطارئ من الطاعات أو المعصية يبقى بحاله ويسقط من السابق بقدره ومذهب ابنه أنه يقابل أجزاء الثواب بأجزاء العقاب فيسقط المتساويان ويبقى الزائد، وعلى هذا يحمل قوله: وقال الجبائي: يحبط من الطاعات السابقة بقدر المعاصي الطارئة من غير أن ينقص من المعاصي شيء أصلاً. فإن بقي له من تلك الطاعات زائداً على قدر المعاصي أثيب به وإلا فلا ولا يخفى أنه تحكم وليس إبطال الطاعات بالمعاصي أي إبطال الطاعات السابقة بمساويه من المعاصي الطارئة أولى من العكس لأنه إبطال أحد المتساويين بالآخر، بل العكس هاهنا أولى لما مر من أن الحسنة تجزى بعشر أمثالها والسيئة لا تجزى إلا بمثلها وقال أبو هاشم بل يوازن بين طاعاته ومعاصيه فأيهما رجح أحبط الآخر وينحبط من الراجح أيضاً ما يساوي مقدار المرجوح ويبقى الزائد فيكون الراجح حينئذ قد أحبط المرجوح على هذا الوجه الذي لا يستلزم ترجيح أحد المتساويين على الآخر.
And the Murji'a held that faith nullifies lapses, so there is no punishment for a lapse along with faith, just as there is no reward for an act of obedience along with disbelief. The Mu'tazila said that one major sin nullifies the reward of all acts of obedience, even if it exceeds his lapse. Al-Jubba'i and his son held to considering the quantity in nullification, claiming that if one's acts of obedience exceed his lapses, they nullify the punishment of his lapses and expiate them; if his lapses exceed his acts of obedience, they nullify the reward of his acts of obedience. Then they differed: al-Jubba'i said: When acts of obedience increase, they nullify all the lapses without anything being diminished from the reward of acts of obedience; when lapses increase, they nullify all the acts of obedience without anything being diminished from the punishment of lapses. Imam al-Razi said: The school of al-Jubba'i is that the incoming acts of obedience or sin remain as they are and drop from the previous according to their measure. The school of his son is that he compares the parts of reward with the parts of punishment; the equal ones fall away and the excess remains. Upon this is interpreted his saying: 'And al-Jubba'i said: It nullifies from the previous acts of obedience in proportion to the incoming sins, without anything being diminished from the sins at all. If there remains for him from those acts of obedience an excess over the amount of sins, he is rewarded by it; otherwise, not. And it is not hidden that this is arbitrary. And it is not that nullification of acts of obedience by sins – i.e., nullification of previous acts of obedience by an equal amount of incoming sins – is more appropriate than the opposite, because it is nullification of one of two equals by the other; rather, the opposite here is more appropriate due to what was mentioned: that a good deed is rewarded tenfold and an evil deed is only requited by its like.' And Abu Hashim said: Rather, he balances between his acts of obedience and his sins; whichever predominates nullifies the other, and from the predominant also falls away what equals the amount of the outweighed, and the excess remains. So the predominant then has nullified the outweighed in this manner, which does not entail giving preference to one of two equals over the other.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanation of the various non-Ash'ari views on nullification (al-ihbat). It first reports the Murji'a position: faith nullifies all sins, so no punishment remains for a believer. Then it restates the Mu'tazila position that a single major sin nullifies all reward. Then it introduces the 'weighting' (ri'ayat al-kathra) school of al-Jubba'i and his son Abu Hashim: they consider the quantity of deeds, not just the presence of sin. Al-Jubba'i's view is that the incoming acts (whether obedience or sin) subtract proportionally from the opposite previous ones: if obedience predominates, it cancels all sin-punishment without reducing its own reward; if sin predominates, it cancels all reward without reducing punishment. Imam al-Razi explains that al-Jubba'i's model is that the incoming remains intact and removes an equal amount from the previous. Abu Hashim's model is a balance (muwazana): he compares the parts of reward and punishment; equal amounts cancel, leaving only the excess. The passage then notes an objection: nullification of previous acts by equal incoming ones is arbitrary; indeed, the opposite (nullifying sins by good deeds) is more appropriate because good deeds are multiplied in reward while evil deeds are not. This sets up the later Ash'ari refutation.

Key Terms
مرجئة
إيمان
زلات
كبيرة
إحباط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Murji'a believe that a believer is never punished at all for any sin.
Correction: The Murji'a hold that faith nullifies the punishment of sins, meaning the believer will not be punished eternally, but they may face temporary punishment before entering Paradise.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Mu'tazila position on nullification?
A) Faith nullifies all sins
B) One major sin nullifies all reward
C) Quantity of deeds determines nullification
D) Sins and good deeds cancel equally

Key Takeaway
The Murji'a and Mu'tazila represent opposite extremes: faith eliminates all sin vs. sin eliminates all good.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever secretly rely on the idea that 'faith alone is enough' to excuse yourself from struggling against a particular sin? How would the Murji'a extreme comfort you—and how might it deceive you from the reality of accountability? Write one action you will take this week to repent sincerely for that sin.
?
When you commit a major sin, do you feel as though all your good deeds are wiped out, leading you to despair? How does that despair mirror the Mu'tazila extreme? This week, when you sin, what specific verse or hadith will you recall to avoid that extreme and seek balanced repentance?
?
Both extremes in this lesson remove the middle ground of repentance and divine mercy. Where in your own heart do you see a tendency toward one of these extremes? What is one concrete step you will take today to anchor yourself in the balanced Sunni understanding that combines hope in Allah’s mercy with fear of His justice?

The Weighting Theory of al-Jubba'i and His Son
Presents the weighting theory of al-Jubba'i and his son.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the common 'weighting' principle of al-Jubba'i and his son on nullification, and how they differed in applying it.

Core Idea
Salvation is a balance scale: weight of deeds determines nullification.

While the Murji'a and Mu'tazila held absolute views, al-Jubba'i and his son introduced a more nuanced 'weighting' approach. They considered the quantity of good and bad deeds. This passage presents their shared principle and then explains the difference between al-Jubba'i and Abu Hashim regarding how nullification operates, as interpreted by Imam al-Razi.

Guided Path
1
Understand the shared principle
Read the first part: 'وذهب الجبائي وابنه إلى رعاية الكثرة في المحبط وزعما أن من زادت طاعاته على زلاته أحبطت عقاب زلاته وكفرتها، ومن زادت زلاته على طاعاته أحبطت ثواب طاعاته'. They both agree that the greater quantity nullifies the lesser.
This is the 'weighting' (رعاية الكثرة) approach.
2
Identify the difference between father and son
Then read: 'ثم اختلفا فقال الجبائي: إذا زادت الطاعات أحبطت الزلات بأسرها ... وقال الإمام الرازي: مذهب الجبائي أن الطارئ من الطاعات أو المعصية يبقى بحاله ويسقط من السابق بقدره ومذهب ابنه أنه يقابل أجزاء الثواب بأجزاء العقاب فيسقط المتساويان ويبقى الزائد'. Al-Jubba'i says the incoming acts remain unchanged and reduce the opposite previous ones; his son says parts of reward and punishment cancel each other equally.
Al-Razi's interpretation clarifies the subtle but important distinction.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider how each model handles equal quantities. According to al-Jubba'i, if incoming acts equal previous ones, what happens? According to his son, what happens?
This will be important for the objection in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهبت المرجئة إلى أن الإيمان يحبط الزلات فلا عقاب على زلة مع الإيمان كما لا ثواب لطاعة مع الكفر، وقالت المعتزلة إن كبيرة واحدة تحبط ثواب جميع الطاعات وإن زادت على زلته وذهب الجبائي وابنه إلى رعاية الكثرة في المحبط وزعما أن من زادت طاعاته على زلاته أحبطت عقاب زلاته وكفرتها، ومن زادت زلاته على طاعاته أحبطت ثواب طاعاته، ثم اختلفا فقال الجبائي: إذا زادت الطاعات أحبطت الزلات بأسرها من غير أن ينقص من ثواب الطاعات شيء، وإذا زادت أحبطت الطاعات برمتها من غير أن ينقص من عقاب الزلات شيء، وقال الإمام الرازي: مذهب الجبائي أن الطارئ من الطاعات أو المعصية يبقى بحاله ويسقط من السابق بقدره ومذهب ابنه أنه يقابل أجزاء الثواب بأجزاء العقاب فيسقط المتساويان ويبقى الزائد، وعلى هذا يحمل قوله: وقال الجبائي: يحبط من الطاعات السابقة بقدر المعاصي الطارئة من غير أن ينقص من المعاصي شيء أصلاً. فإن بقي له من تلك الطاعات زائداً على قدر المعاصي أثيب به وإلا فلا ولا يخفى أنه تحكم وليس إبطال الطاعات بالمعاصي أي إبطال الطاعات السابقة بمساويه من المعاصي الطارئة أولى من العكس لأنه إبطال أحد المتساويين بالآخر، بل العكس هاهنا أولى لما مر من أن الحسنة تجزى بعشر أمثالها والسيئة لا تجزى إلا بمثلها وقال أبو هاشم بل يوازن بين طاعاته ومعاصيه فأيهما رجح أحبط الآخر وينحبط من الراجح أيضاً ما يساوي مقدار المرجوح ويبقى الزائد فيكون الراجح حينئذ قد أحبط المرجوح على هذا الوجه الذي لا يستلزم ترجيح أحد المتساويين على الآخر.
And the Murji'a held that faith nullifies lapses, so there is no punishment for a lapse along with faith, just as there is no reward for an act of obedience along with disbelief. The Mu'tazila said that one major sin nullifies the reward of all acts of obedience, even if it exceeds his lapse. Al-Jubba'i and his son held to considering the quantity in nullification, claiming that if one's acts of obedience exceed his lapses, they nullify the punishment of his lapses and expiate them; if his lapses exceed his acts of obedience, they nullify the reward of his acts of obedience. Then they differed: al-Jubba'i said: When acts of obedience increase, they nullify all the lapses without anything being diminished from the reward of acts of obedience; when lapses increase, they nullify all the acts of obedience without anything being diminished from the punishment of lapses. Imam al-Razi said: The school of al-Jubba'i is that the incoming acts of obedience or sin remain as they are and drop from the previous according to their measure. The school of his son is that he compares the parts of reward with the parts of punishment; the equal ones fall away and the excess remains. Upon this is interpreted his saying: 'And al-Jubba'i said: It nullifies from the previous acts of obedience in proportion to the incoming sins, without anything being diminished from the sins at all. If there remains for him from those acts of obedience an excess over the amount of sins, he is rewarded by it; otherwise, not. And it is not hidden that this is arbitrary. And it is not that nullification of acts of obedience by sins – i.e., nullification of previous acts of obedience by an equal amount of incoming sins – is more appropriate than the opposite, because it is nullification of one of two equals by the other; rather, the opposite here is more appropriate due to what was mentioned: that a good deed is rewarded tenfold and an evil deed is only requited by its like.' And Abu Hashim said: Rather, he balances between his acts of obedience and his sins; whichever predominates nullifies the other, and from the predominant also falls away what equals the amount of the outweighed, and the excess remains. So the predominant then has nullified the outweighed in this manner, which does not entail giving preference to one of two equals over the other.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanation of the various non-Ash'ari views on nullification (al-ihbat). It first reports the Murji'a position: faith nullifies all sins, so no punishment remains for a believer. Then it restates the Mu'tazila position that a single major sin nullifies all reward. Then it introduces the 'weighting' (ri'ayat al-kathra) school of al-Jubba'i and his son Abu Hashim: they consider the quantity of deeds, not just the presence of sin. Al-Jubba'i's view is that the incoming acts (whether obedience or sin) subtract proportionally from the opposite previous ones: if obedience predominates, it cancels all sin-punishment without reducing its own reward; if sin predominates, it cancels all reward without reducing punishment. Imam al-Razi explains that al-Jubba'i's model is that the incoming remains intact and removes an equal amount from the previous. Abu Hashim's model is a balance (muwazana): he compares the parts of reward and punishment; equal amounts cancel, leaving only the excess. The passage then notes an objection: nullification of previous acts by equal incoming ones is arbitrary; indeed, the opposite (nullifying sins by good deeds) is more appropriate because good deeds are multiplied in reward while evil deeds are not. This sets up the later Ash'ari refutation.

Key Terms
الجبائي
الموازنة
أجزاء الثواب
أجزاء العقاب
المتساويان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Jubba'i and his son held identical views on nullification.
Correction: They shared the weighting principle but differed on the mechanism: al-Jubba'i's model is asymmetrical (incoming remains, previous is reduced), while Abu Hashim's is symmetrical (equal parts cancel).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the difference between al-Jubba'i and his son regarding nullification?
A) Al-Jubba'i says incoming acts remain and subtract from previous; his son says parts cancel equally.
B) Al-Jubba'i says parts cancel equally; his son says incoming remains and subtracts.
C) Both hold the same view but differ on terminology.
D) Al-Jubba'i ignores quantity; his son considers it.

Key Takeaway
Al-Jubba'i and his son agree on weighting by quantity but differ on the mechanism of cancellation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Jubba'i's model suggests that if your good deeds outweigh your sins, your sins are completely wiped out without reducing your reward. Does this thought make you more complacent about sinning, or does it motivate you to increase your good deeds? What will you do differently today because of this?
?
Abu Hashim's model cancels equal parts of reward and punishment. If you imagine your life as a ledger, which model makes you feel more accountable for each individual action? Why does that matter for how you live tomorrow?
?
Both scholars agreed that your balance ultimately matters. If you were to die right now, would your scale tip more heavily towards good or bad? What is one concrete change you need to make this week to shift that balance?

The Objection and Abu Hashim's Alternative
Presents objection against al-Jubba'i and Abu Hashim's alternative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection against al-Jubba'i's model regarding arbitrariness, and how Abu Hashim's balancing model avoids it.

Core Idea
A proper theory must not arbitrarily cancel one of two equal things.

After presenting the weighting theory, the passage raises a critical objection: it seems arbitrary to cancel previous acts by equal incoming acts, especially since good deeds are rewarded tenfold while evil deeds are only punished equally. This objection opens the door for Abu Hashim's alternative balancing model, which aims to treat both sides fairly.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the part starting from 'ولا يخفى أنه تحكم' up to 'بل العكس هاهنا أولى لما مر من أن الحسنة تجزى بعشر أمثالها والسيئة لا تجزى إلا بمثلها'. The objection points out that nullifying previous acts by equal incoming acts is arbitrary and that the opposite (nullifying sins by good deeds) is more appropriate because good deeds are rewarded tenfold.
The multiplier (الحسنة بعشر أمثالها) is a key point.
2
Understand Abu Hashim's alternative
Read the final part: 'وقال أبو هاشم بل يوازن بين طاعاته ومعاصيه فأيهما رجح أحبط الآخر وينحبط من الراجح أيضاً ما يساوي مقدار المرجوح ويبقى الزائد'. Abu Hashim proposes a balancing (موازنة) where the predominant nullifies the other, but also an equal amount from the predominant is nullified, leaving only the excess.
This avoids the charge of arbitrariness because both sides are treated symmetrically.
3
Compare with al-Jubba'i's model
Consider how Abu Hashim's model differs from his father's: does it escape the objection? Why or why not?
The passage ends by noting Abu Hashim's model does not entail preferring one equal over another.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Abu Hashim's balancing model is identical to al-Jubba'i's except it cancels only the excess.
Correction: Abu Hashim's model involves mutual cancellation: both sides lose equal amounts, whereas al-Jubba'i's model only reduces the previous side while the incoming remains untouched.

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hashim, what happens to the predominating side when it nullifies the outweighed side?
A) The predominating side remains entirely intact.
B) The predominating side is also reduced by an amount equal to the outweighed side.
C) The predominating side is completely cancelled.
D) The predominating side loses half its value.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hashim's balancing model resolves the arbitrariness of al-Jubba'i's model by cancelling equal parts from both sides, leaving only the surplus.

?
Al-Jubba'i's model arbitrarily cancels good deeds by sins. Abu Hashim offers a balanced mutual cancellation. Which model better reflects your understanding of Allah's justice? Why? What does this mean for your own accountability?
?
If you truly believed that every sin could cancel a good deed, how would your daily actions change? Would you be more vigilant, or would you despair? How does Abu Hashim's 'excess remaining' (الزائد) give you hope?
?
The objection in this passage is that preferring one of two equals without reason is arbitrary. In your own life, do you sometimes treat equal situations differently without cause? How can this theological principle guide you to seek justice in your own decisions?

Al-Jubba'i's View on Nullification and Its Critique
Al-Jubba'i states his view on nullification and the critique against it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Jubba'i's theory of nullification of acts of obedience by sins and the critique that it is arbitrary.

Core Idea
Al-Jubba'i's nullification theory is arbitrary because good deeds are rewarded tenfold.

This passage presents the view of al-Jubba'i on how sins can nullify the reward of previous acts of obedience. It explains his rule, his son's modification, and then critiques it as arbitrary, arguing that the opposite (acts of obedience nullifying sins) is more plausible given the tenfold reward.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that al-Jubba'i presents two symmetrical cases.
2
Understand al-Jubba'i's rule
Identify the two scenarios: when acts of obedience increase and when sins increase. Note what happens to the other type of act.
3
Examine al-Razi's explanation
Al-Razi clarifies that the new act cancels an equal amount from the previous act. Also note the son's modification.
This shows the internal logic of the madhhab.
4
Grasp the critique
The passage calls the nullification arbitrary and explains why the opposite (obedience nullifying sins) is more appropriate due to the tenfold reward of good deeds.
This is the key theological point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجبائي : إذا زادت الطاعات أحبطت الزلات بأسرها من غير أن ينقص من ثواب الطاعات شيء، وإذا زادت أحبطت الطاعات برمتها من غير أن ينقص من عقاب الزلات شيء، وقال الإمام الرازي: مذهب الجبائي أن الطارئ من الطاعات أو المعصية يبقى بحاله ويسقط من السابق بقدره ومذهب ابنه أنه يقابل أجزاء الثواب بأجزاء العقاب فيسقط المتساويان ويبقى الزائد، وعلى هذا يحمل قوله : ( وقال الجبائي : يحبط من الطاعات ) أي السابقة ( بقدر المعاصي الطارئة من غير أن ينقص من المعاصي شيء أصلاً . فإن بقي له من تلك الطاعات زائداً على قدر المعاصي أثيب به وإلا فلا ولا يخفى أنه تحكم وليس إبطال الطاعات بالمعاصي ) أي إبطال الطاعات السابقة بمساويه من المعاصي الطارئة ( أولى من العكس ) لأنه إبطال أحد المتساويين بالآخر، بل العكس هاهنا أولى لما مر من أن الحسنة تجزى بعشر أمثالها والسيئة لا تجزى إلا بمثلها
Al-Jubba'i: If acts of obedience increase, they nullify all the slips entirely without anything decreasing from the reward of the acts of obedience; and if (slips) increase, they nullify all the acts of obedience entirely without anything decreasing from the punishment of the slips. And al-Imam al-Razi said: The madhhab of al-Jubba'i is that the newly occurring (act) of obedience or disobedience remains in its state and falls from the previous (act) by its amount, and the madhhab of his son is that he makes the parts of reward correspond to the parts of punishment, so the two equals fall and the surplus remains. And upon this is interpreted his statement: 'And al-Jubba'i said: He nullifies from the acts of obedience' i.e., the previous ones, 'by the amount of the sins' i.e., the newly occurring ones, without anything decreasing from the sins at all. So if there remains for him from those acts of obedience a surplus over the amount of the sins, he is rewarded thereby; otherwise not. And it is not hidden that it is arbitrary. And 'the nullification of acts of obedience by sins' i.e., the nullification of previous acts of obedience by an equal amount of newly occurring sins 'is not more appropriate than the opposite' because it is a nullification of one of two equals by the other. Rather, the opposite here is 'more appropriate, due to what has passed that a good deed is rewarded tenfold and an evil deed is only recompensed with its like.'
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage defines the view of al-Jubba'i on the nullification of acts of obedience by sins. It begins by stating his position: if acts of obedience increase, they nullify all slips without reducing reward; if sins increase, they nullify all acts of obedience without reducing punishment. Then it explains that the newly occurring act (whether obedience or sin) remains as it is and subtracts from the previous one by its amount. Al-Jubba'i's son modifies this by comparing equal parts of reward and punishment, canceling the equal portions and leaving the surplus. The criticism follows: the claim that a later sin nullifies an earlier act of obedience by an equal amount is arbitrary. The opposite nullification (i.e., that an earlier act of obedience nullifies a later sin) is more appropriate because a good deed is rewarded tenfold, so a single good deed outweighs multiple sins. Thus, nullifying many sins with one good deed is more fitting than the reverse.

Key Terms
إحباط
طاعات
معاصي
ثواب
عقاب
تحكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Al-Jubba'i's nullification theory is arbitrary without any reasoning.
Correction: Al-Jubba'i's theory has a logical structure: new acts cancel equal amounts from previous ones. However, the critique is that it is arbitrary because it ignores the tenfold reward of good deeds, making the opposite more plausible.

Checkpoint
According to al-Jubba'i, if a person's sins increase, what happens to the reward from previous acts of obedience?
A) It is completely nullified.
B) It is partially nullified.
C) It remains intact.
D) It is increased.

Key Takeaway
Al-Jubba'i's nullification theory is critiqued as arbitrary because it assigns equal weight to sins and acts of obedience, ignoring that good deeds are rewarded tenfold.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that a single good deed can outweigh multiple sins because of God's tenfold reward, why do you still hesitate to perform small acts of obedience? What sin are you holding onto that you could let go of by starting with one sincere good deed today?
?
The critique here shows that al-Jubba'i's 'balanced' view is actually arbitrary — it ignores God's mercy. Do you live as if your good deeds and sins are weighed equally? Or do you rely on God's promise of multiplying reward to tip the scales? What one habit can you change this week to increase your deeds knowing they are multiplied?
?
Al-Jubba'i's theory nullifies entire categories of deeds based on the last action. How does this reflect the human tendency to despair after sinning? How can you use the tenfold reward to counteract hopelessness and actively seek forgiveness and good deeds instead of feeling that your past good deeds are wasted?
