اشتراط
نصب الإمام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The three conditions are universally accepted by all scholars.
Correction: The passage shows a strong objection that these conditions are not binding because they are rarely found together.

Checkpoint
What is the main objection against stipulating the first three conditions for the imam?
A) They are impossible to fulfill.
B) They are not found together nowadays, leading to logical problems.
C) The Prophet did not possess them.
D) The imamate can exist without them by consensus.

Key Takeaway
The first three conditions for imam are disputed because they are rarely found together, making their stipulation either futile or impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you evaluate the leaders in your community, do you impose conditions that you yourself fail to meet? What would it cost you to lower your expectations and still support them?
?
The Prophet stood alone after the Muslims fled, declaring his identity. In what area of your life are you tempted to retreat from your principles? What would it mean for you to stand firm and say 'I am a believer, no lie'?
?
The objection warns that demanding perfection leads to impossible burdens. Are there standards you place on yourself or others that God never required? How can you distinguish between a genuine duty and a self-imposed ideal that excuses inaction?

The Five Consensual Conditions for the Imam
Lists the five conditions for imam with rationales.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five conditions for the imam that are agreed upon by consensus and the rationales behind each.

Core Idea
The imam must be just, intelligent, adult, male, and free — these are the five consensual conditions.

This passage from al-Mawaqif lists the five conditions for the imam that all Sunni scholars agree upon. Each condition is accompanied by a brief rationale explaining why it is necessary for the proper functioning of the caliphate. The author also notes that when added to previous conditions, the total reaches eight, but the consensus is on these five.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice how each condition is stated and immediately followed by a short reason.
The passage is concise; each condition is justified in a few words.
2
Identify the five conditions
List the five conditions in order: justice, intellect, adulthood, masculinity, freedom. For each, find the rationale given in the text.
The conditions are labelled clearly in the translation.
3
Reflect on the rationale
Consider why each condition is necessary for the imam. For example, justice prevents oppression, intellect enables proper governance, etc.
These rationales reflect the practical concerns of leadership in classical Islamic thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نعم يجب أن يكون عدلاً) في الظاهر ( لئلا يجور) فإن الفاسق ربما يصرف الأموال في أغراض نفسه فيضيع الحقوق ) عاقلا ليصلح للتصرفات ) الشرعية والملكية (بالغاً لقصور عقل الصبي ذكراً إذ النساء ناقصات عقل ودين، حراً لئلا يشغله خدمة السيد ) عن وظائف الإمامة ) ولئلا يحتقر فيعصى) فإن الأحرار يستحقرون العبيد ويستنكفون عن طاعتهم (فهذه الصفات) التي هي الثمان أو الخمس ( شروط) معتبرة في الإمامة ( بالإجماع
Yes, it is necessary that he be just in outward appearance, lest he act unjustly, for the transgressor might spend funds for his own purposes and waste rights. (He must be) intelligent to be fit for legal and royal dispositions, an adult due to the deficiency of a child's mind, male since women are deficient in intellect and religion, free so that the service of a master does not occupy him from the functions of the imamate and so that he is not despised and thereby disobeyed—for free men despise slaves and disdain obeying them. So these attributes, which are eight or five, are conditions considered in the imamate by consensus.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage enumerates the five conditions for the imam that are agreed upon by consensus: justice, intellect, adulthood, masculinity, and freedom. The author provides brief rationales: justice prevents oppression, intellect allows proper management, adulthood avoids childish deficiency, maleness because women are considered deficient in intellect and religion, and freedom to prevent preoccupation with a master and to ensure respect. The author notes that when combined with the previous three, the total is eight, but the consensus is on these five.

بلوغ
ذكورة
حرية
إجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These conditions are universally accepted in all Islamic political thought.
Correction: While they are agreed upon by Sunni consensus, they are specific to the Sunni theological tradition; other sects may have different conditions.

Checkpoint

C) Because only free men can be knowledgeable.
D) Because the Prophet said so.

Key Takeaway
The five consensual conditions for the imam are justice, intellect, adulthood, masculinity, and freedom, each with a practical rationale.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The text says a just imam is needed because a transgressor might spend funds for his own purposes. Where in your life are you entrusted with resources (time, money, influence) that you are using for selfish gain rather than for the right of Allah? What step will you take today to repent and correct that misuse?
?
The condition of adulthood warns of a child's deficient mind. In what areas of your spiritual and worldly responsibilities are you acting immaturely—avoiding accountability or making decisions based on whims? What one practice can you adopt this week to grow in maturity?
?
Freedom is required so that no master's service occupies the imam. What 'master'—a job pursuit, a relationship, a habit, or love of status—is occupying you from fulfilling your duties to Allah and to those under your care? How can you free yourself from that attachment before the next Friday prayer?

The Qurayshi Condition and Its Evidence
Presents the Qurayshi condition and its evidence from hadith and consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scriptural and consensus-based evidence for the condition that the imam must be from Quraysh.

Core Idea
The imam must be Qurayshi, as proven by the Prophet's hadith and the Companions' consensus.

This lesson presents the first disputed condition for the imamate: being from the Quraysh tribe. The author cites the famous hadith 'The imams are from Quraysh' and shows how the Companions, including Abu Bakr, used it at Saqifah to resolve the succession dispute, thereby establishing a binding consensus.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the parties involved: Ash'aris and Jubba'is versus Kharijites and some Mu'tazilites.
The passage is from a longer work on Islamic theology.
2
Identify the evidence
The author gives two pieces of evidence: the Prophet's statement and the Companions' acceptance of it as a decisive proof at Saqifah.
This shows that the condition is not merely theoretical but was applied in early Islamic history.
3
Understand the term 'Qurayshi'
Reflect on why the Quraysh tribe is specified. The commentary explains that this is based on scriptural and historical consensus, not racial preference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهاهنا صفات) أخرى ( في اشتراطها خلاف الأولى : أن يكون قرشياً) اشترطه الأشاعرة والجبائيان ) ومنعه الخوارج وبعض المعتزلة لنا قوله عليه السلام « الأئمة قریش ثم إن الصحابة عملوا بمضمون هذ الحديث ) فإن أبا بكر رضي استدل به يوم السقيفة على الأنصار حين نازعوا في الإمامة بمحضر الصحابة فقبلوه وأجمعوا عليه فصار) دليلاً قاطعاً) يفيد اليقين باشتراط القرشية (احتجوا) أي من منعوا تلك الصفات ( بقوله عليه السلام السمع والطاعة ولو عبداً حبشياً) فإنه يدل على أن الإمام قد لا يكون قرشياً (قلنا : ذلك الحديث فيمن أمره الإمام) أي جعله أميراً ( على سرية وغيرها كناحية، ويجب حمله على هذا دفعاً للتعارض بينه وبين الإجماع أو نقول : هو مبالغة على سبيل الفرض ويدل عليه أنه لا يجوز كون الإمام عبداً إجماعاً
And here are other attributes concerning whose stipulation there is disagreement. First: that he be Qurayshi. The Ash'aris and the two Jubba'is stipulated it, while the Kharijites and some of the Mu'tazilites denied it. Our evidence: His (peace be upon him) saying: 'The imams are from Quraysh.' Then the Companions acted upon the content of this hadith, for Abu Bakr (may God be pleased with him) used it as evidence in the day of Saqifah against the Ansar when they disputed about the imamate, in the presence of the Companions, and they accepted it and agreed upon it, so it became a conclusive proof giving certainty about stipulating Quraysh. They (the opponents) argued with his saying: 'Hearing and obedience, even if (the commander) is an Abyssinian slave,' for it indicates that the imam may not be Qurayshi. We say: That hadith refers to one whom the imam appoints as an amir over a detachment or similar, and it must be interpreted this way to avoid contradiction with the consensus; or we say: It is an exaggeration by way of hypothesis, and it is indicated by the consensus that the imam cannot be a slave.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage presents the condition that the imam must be from the Quraysh tribe. The author notes the parties: Ash'aris and Jubba'is stipulate it, Kharijites and some Mu'tazilites deny it. Evidence adduced is the Prophet's hadith 'The imams are from Quraysh' and the fact that the Companions used it at Saqifah to settle the dispute, achieving consensus. The objectors cite the hadith 'Hearing and obedience even if an Abyssinian slave.' The response: that hadith refers to appointed commanders, not the supreme imam, or it is hypothetical exaggeration, as the impossibility of a slave imam is agreed upon.

Misconception: That the Qurayshi condition is a racial or tribal preference unrelated to religion.

Key Takeaway
The Qurayshi condition for the imam is established by the Prophet's hadith and the consensus of the Companions.

?

?

Responding to the Objection from the Abyssinian Slave Hadith
Responds to the objection from the hadith 'Hearing and obedience even if an Abyssinian slave.'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author responds to the objection based on the hadith 'Hearing and obedience even if an Abyssinian slave.'

Core Idea
The hadith about obeying an Abyssinian slave refers to appointed commanders, not the supreme imam.

The opponents of the Qurayshi condition cite a hadith that seems to allow any commander, even an Abyssinian slave. This lesson examines the author's two responses: either the hadith is about subordinate amirs, or it is a hypothetical exaggeration. The consensus that a slave cannot be imam supports this interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic text of the objection and the response. Note how the opponents use the hadith 'Hearing and obedience even if an Abyssinian slave.'
This hadith is often quoted to argue against any condition on lineage.
2
Analyze the first response
The author says the hadith refers to someone the imam appoints as an amir, not the imam himself. This removes the contradiction.
3
Analyze the second response
Alternatively, the hadith is an exaggeration for emphasis, as it is agreed that a slave cannot be imam.
This shows the limits of literal interpretation.

Three Disputed Conditions for Imam and Their Refutation
Lists conditions and rebuts them using Abu Bakr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three conditions for imam proposed by Shi'a and other groups, and how the Sunni refutation using the example of Abu Bakr invalidates them.

Core Idea
The imamate of Abu Bakr disproves the need for being Hashimi, a scholar, or a miracle-worker.

In this passage, the author presents three additional conditions for the imam that are not required by the Sunni consensus. These conditions—being from the Hashimite clan, being a scholar of all religious matters, and performing miracles—are advanced by various sects. The rebuttal is straightforward: Abu Bakr, the first caliph, possessed none of these, yet his imamate is established by consensus.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three conditions
Read the Arabic passage and find the three conditions listed. Notice which group stipulates each: being Hashimi (Shi'a), being a scholar (Imamiyya), and performing miracles (extremists).
The passage numbers them as second, third, and fourth attributes.
2
Understand the rebuttal
The author uses Abu Bakr as a counterexample. Reflect on how each condition fails for Abu Bakr: he was not Hashimi, not a scholar of all religious matters in actuality, and did not perform miracles. Yet his imamate is valid.
The author says 'we will shortly demonstrate the imamate of Abu Bakr.'
3
Appreciate the argument's force
The consensus on Abu Bakr's imamate implies these conditions are not necessary. Consider the logical structure: if a necessary condition is absent, the claim cannot hold; but since Abu Bakr's imamate is valid, these conditions cannot be necessary.
This argument relies on the established Sunni consensus regarding Abu Bakr's rightful caliphate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثانية : ( من تلك الصفات أن يكون هاشمياً، شرطه الشيعة. الثالثة : : ) أن يكون عالماً مسائل الدين أصولها وفروها بالفعل لا بالقوة ( وقد شرطه بجميع الإمامية الرابعة ظهور المعجزة على يده إذ به يعلم صدقه في دعوى الإمامة والعصمة، وبه قال الغلاة ؛ ويبطل هذه الثلاثة ) واشتراطها في الإمامة ( أنا ندل ) عن قريب ( على خلافه أبي بكر ) وكونه إماماً حقاً ) ولا يجب له شيء مما ذكر) من الأوصاف فإن كونه هاشمياً ممتنع والآخرين لا يجبان له إجماعاً
Second: (Among those attributes) that he be Hashimi—the Shi'a stipulated it. Third: That he be a scholar of the matters of religion, its principles and branches, in actuality not potentiality—all the Imamiyya stipulated it. Fourth: The appearance of a miracle at his hand, for thereby his truthfulness in claiming the imamate and infallibility is known—the extremists said this. And these three (conditions) and their stipulation in the imamate are invalidated by the fact that we will shortly demonstrate the imamate of Abu Bakr and that he was a true imam, and none of these mentioned attributes are necessary for him; for his being Hashimi is impossible, and the other two are not obligatory for him by consensus.
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage lists three additional conditions for the imam proposed by various sects: being Hashimi (Shi'a), being a scholar in actuality (Imamiyya), and performing miracles (extremists). The author rebuts them by pointing to Abu Bakr's imamate: he was not Hashimi (since he was not from Banu Hashim), nor was he required to be a scholar in actuality or perform miracles. The consensus on Abu Bakr's rightful imamate proves these conditions are not necessary.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that these conditions (Hashimi, scholar, miracle-worker) are required by all Muslims for imamate.
Correction: The text shows these conditions are specific to Shi'a and extremist groups, and the Sunni consensus on Abu Bakr's imamate proves they are not necessary.

Checkpoint
How does the author invalidate the condition of being Hashimi?
A) By citing a hadith.
B) By noting that Abu Bakr was not Hashimi yet was imam.
C) By consensus that only Quraysh matters.
D) By logical argument.

Key Takeaway
The conditions of being Hashimi, a scholar in actuality, and a miracle-worker are not necessary for imamate, as demonstrated by Abu Bakr's valid imamate lacking all three.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you evaluate leaders in your community, do you subconsciously demand qualifications that the Shari'ah does not require? Reflect on a leader you respect: do you value them more for their lineage or appearance of piety? How can you apply the lesson of Abu Bakr to prioritize character over worldly credentials?
?
The extremists demanded miracles as proof of imamate. In your own spiritual life, do you ever demand signs from God before submitting to a truth that is already clear from the Qur'an and Sunnah? What ordinary proofs have you overlooked recently?
?
Abu Bakr was chosen despite lacking scholarly reputation and tribal descent. Are you willing to follow leaders who are humble and unassuming, or do you only trust those with impressive titles and accomplishments? What does this say about the state of your heart?

The Condition of Infallibility and Its Refutation
Presents the condition of infallibility, two proofs, and refutations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Imami/Ismaili condition of infallibility for the imam and how the author refutes it using two counterarguments.

Core Idea
Infallibility is not a requirement for imamate; the Quranic verse refers to unrepentant sinners, not non-infallibles.

This passage tackles a key sectarian claim: that the imam must be infallible. The author presents the Imami and Ismaili position, then refutes it by appealing to consensus on Abu Bakr and by addressing two scriptural and rational proofs. You will see how theological debate relies on precise definitions and logical refutations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument flow naturally.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the condition and its proponents
Note that the Imamiyya and Isma'iliyya stipulate infallibility. The author immediately refutes by pointing to Abu Bakr.
This shows the condition is not universally accepted.
3
Examine the first proof and its refutation
The opponents argue that the imam either teaches divine sciences or prevents error. The author denies both purposes and redefines the need for an imam.
The refutation cuts the root of the argument.
4
Examine the second proof and its refutation
The opponents cite Quran 2:124. The author narrows the meaning of 'wrongdoer' to unrepentant sinners.
Precise definition is key here.
5
Summarize the author's position
Combine the refutations: infallibility is not necessary; the Quranic verse does not exclude non-infallible righteous persons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامسة : أن يكون معصوماً، شرطها الإمامية والإسماعيلية؛ ويبطله أن أبا بكر لا تجب عصمته اتفاقاً) ثبوت إمامته ( احتجوا) على اشتراط العصمة ) بوجهين : الأول : إن مع الحاجة إلى الإمام إما للتعليم أي لتعليم الناس المعارف الإلهية كما ذهب إليه الملاحدة ) ولو جاز جهله وعدم عصمته ( لما صلح لذلك ) ولم يفد تعليمه اليقين إذ يجوز حينئذ خطأ فيما علم . ( وإما لجواز الخطأ على غيره في الأحكام كما ذهب إليه الإمامية . ( فلو جاز ) الخطأ ( عليه أيضاً لم يحصل الغرض ) منه بل احتاج إلى إمام ،آخر ويتسلسل الجواب منع كون الحاجة إليه لأحدهما، بل لما تقدم من دفع الضرر والمظنون (الثاني : ( من الوجهين قوله تعالى : لا ينال عهدي الظالمين ﴾ [البقرة : ١٢٤ ] ، في جواب إبراهيم عليه السلام حين طلب الإمامة لذريته ) وغير المعصوم ظالم فلا يناله عهد الإمامة . الجواب : لا نسلم أن الظالم من ليس بمعصوم بل من ارتكب معصية مسقطة للعدالة مع عدم التوبة والإصلاح
Fifth: That he be infallible—the Imamiyya and Isma'iliyya stipulated it. And it is invalidated by the fact that Abu Bakr's infallibility is not necessary by agreement, (yet) his imamate is established. They argued for stipulating infallibility with two proofs. First: The need for the imam is either for teaching divine sciences (as the mulahida hold), and if his ignorance and lack of infallibility were allowed, he would not be fit for that, nor would his teaching give certainty, for error in what he teaches would be possible; or (the need is) for the possibility of error in others regarding rulings (as the Imamiyya hold), and if error were also possible for him, the purpose would not be achieved; rather, another imam would be needed, leading to an infinite regress. The answer: We deny that the need is for either of these; rather, it is for what was previously mentioned: repelling harm and speculation. Second: His saying, exalted: 'My covenant does not include the wrongdoers' (Quran 2:124), in response to Abraham (peace be upon him) when he requested the imamate for his progeny; and the non-infallible is a wrongdoer, so he will not attain the covenant of imamate. The answer: We do not grant that the wrongdoer is one who is not infallible; rather, it is one who commits a sin that removes uprightness without repentance and reform.
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage discusses the condition of infallibility (ismah) for the imam, stipulated by the Imamiyya and Isma'iliyya. The author refutes it by stating that Abu Bakr's imamate is established by consensus even though his infallibility is not required. The opponents present two proofs: (1) the need for the imam is either for teaching or for preventing error in judgments, and an infallible imam is necessary to achieve certainty or avoid regress; (2) Quran 2:124 'My covenant does not include the wrongdoers,' and non-infallible persons are wrongdoers. The author answers the first by denying that the need is for those purposes; rather, the need for an imam is for repelling harm and speculation as previously established. The answer to the second is that 'wrongdoer' in the verse refers to one who commits a sin that removes uprightness, not to anyone who is not infallible.

Key Terms
عصمة
إمامية
إسماعيلية
لا ينال عهدي الظالمين
إبراهيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quranic verse 'My covenant does not include the wrongdoers' means anyone who is not infallible is a wrongdoer.
Correction: The author clarifies that 'wrongdoer' refers specifically to one who commits a sin removing uprightness without repentance, not to all non-infallible persons.

Checkpoint
How does the author answer the proof from the verse 'My covenant does not include the wrongdoers'?
A) By saying that the verse is abrogated.
B) By defining 'wrongdoer' as one who commits a sin that removes uprightness without repentance.
C) By claiming that the imam is not included in the covenant.
D) By stating that infallibility is not required for the covenant.

Key Takeaway
Infallibility is not a necessary condition for imamate; the opponents' proofs are refuted by redefining the purpose of the imam and the meaning of 'wrongdoer'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you find yourself demanding perfection from those you follow, forgetting that Allah accepts the repentance of the righteous who err? How does this lesson challenge your tendency to dismiss someone's leadership because of a sin they committed?
?
The author redefines 'wrongdoer' to exclude the repentant. Have you ever labeled someone as a 'wrongdoer' in your heart after a single mistake, ignoring their repentance and reform? What would it mean to instead view them as Allah views them—as one who has turned back to Him?
?
If you were in a position of leadership, how would knowing that infallibility is not required affect your humility and reliance on Allah? Are you willing to lead even knowing you will make mistakes, trusting in His guidance and forgiveness?

The Necessity of Designation for Imamate
Introduces the requirement of nass for imamate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Shia view insists on explicit designation (nass) as the sole legitimate basis for imamate, rejecting mere qualification or election.

Core Idea
Imamate requires divine designation, not just personal merit.

This passage opens the discussion of how the imamate is established. It immediately rejects the notion that simply being qualified makes someone an imam. Instead, it introduces the critical element of explicit designation (nass) from the Prophet or the preceding imam. This reflects a core Shia doctrine that distinguishes it from Sunni views on leadership.

Guided Path
1
Read the original Arabic
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice that it begins with 'The third objective' and then states the main claim: a person does not become imam merely by being fit. Try to identify the key phrase that indicates what is necessary.
The passage is cut off, but the point is clear.
2
Focus on the key principle
Identify the negation: 'mere suitability does not make one imam.' Then the affirmation: 'it requires textual designation.' This is the heart of the lesson.
3
Consider the implications
Ask yourself: Why would mere qualification be insufficient? How does this principle shape the Shia understanding of leadership?
This connects to the broader difference between Shia and Sunni views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The third objective: Concerning what establishes the imamate. (As for what establishes the imamate,) a person does not become imam merely by being fit for the imamate and possessing its conditions; rather, another matter is necessary for that. And it is established by textual designation from the Messenger and from
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage introduces the third objective of the chapter: how the imamate is established. The author states that mere possession of the qualifications does not make one imam; there must be an additional factor: designation (nass) by the Prophet or the previous imam. The text is cut off before completing the sentence, but the main point is clear: imamate is not simply by appointment or election; it requires explicit designation.

Misconception: Some think that anyone who meets the conditions of an imam (knowledge, justice, etc.) can validly become imam without further authorization.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that mere suitability is insufficient and that textual designation from the Prophet or previous imam is necessary.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about following a leader in your faith, do you tend to trust your own judgment about who is qualified, or do you submit to what Allah has designated? Which choice gives you more peace?
?
This lesson teaches that even the most qualified person does not become imam without divine appointment. Is there an area in your life where you rely on your own qualifications instead of seeking Allah's designation? How can you realign?
?
If you genuinely believe that the Prophet (peace be upon him) designated specific leaders after him, what is one practical step you will take this week to align your heart and actions with that truth?

Refuting the Three Shi'a Conditions for Imamate
Sanusi lists three Shi'a conditions and refutes them via Abu Bakr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes three Shi'a conditions for the imamate by appealing to the consensus on Abu Bakr's legitimate imamate despite his lack of these attributes.

Core Idea
Abu Bakr's imamate refutes the need for Hashimi lineage, comprehensive knowledge, and miracles.

In this passage, Sanusi continues his discussion of the attributes required for an imam. He lists three conditions proposed by various Shi'a groups—being from the Hashimi clan, possessing actual comprehensive knowledge of religion, and performing miracles—and then dismisses them by pointing to the recognized imamate of Abu Bakr, who lacked all three. This argument relies on the consensus that Abu Bakr was indeed the rightful caliph, despite not meeting these criteria.

Guided Path
1
Read and translate
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Notice the concise listing of conditions and the strong refutation.
The passage uses 'الثانية' and 'الثالثة' to continue a list started earlier.
2
Identify the three conditions
Find the three conditions given: being Hashimi, being knowledgeable (actual knowledge), and performing miracles. Note which groups hold each.
The Shi'a are mentioned for the first, all Imamiyya for the second, and the extremists (ghulat) for the third.
3
Understand the refutation
Sanusi invalidates all three by stating that Abu Bakr was the true imam despite lacking them. He explicitly says being Hashimi is impossible for him, and the other two are not obligatory by consensus.
This is a deductive argument: the recognized imamate of Abu Bakr contradicts the necessity of these conditions.
4
Reflect on the argument's strength
Consider why this is a powerful argument in Sunni kalam: it uses a historical consensus (ijma) to refute a theological claim. Would this convince a Shi'a opponent? Why or why not?
The argument assumes the legitimacy of Abu Bakr's caliphate is agreed upon, which Shi'a deny.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثانية : من تلك الصفات أن يكون هاشمياً، شرطه الشيعة. الثالثة : أن يكون عالماً بمسائل الدين أصولها وفروعها بالفعل لا بالقوة، وقد شرطه جميع الإمامية. الرابعة : ظهور المعجزة على يده إذ به يعلم صدقه في دعوى الإمامة والعصمة، وبه قال الغلاة. ويبطل هذه الثلاثة واشتراطها في الإمامة أنا ندل عن قريب على خلافه أبي بكر وكونه إماماً حقاً ولا يجب له شيء مما ذكر من الأوصاف فإن كونه هاشمياً ممتنع والآخرين لا يجبان له إجماعاً.
The second: among those attributes is that he be Hashimi; the Shi'a stipulate it. The third: that he be knowledgeable in the matters of religion, its principles and branches, in actuality not in potentiality; all of the Imamiyya stipulate it. The fourth: the appearance of a miracle at his hand, for by it his truthfulness in the claim of imamate and infallibility is known; the extremists hold this. And these three are invalidated – and stipulating them in imamate – by the fact that we will soon prove that Abu Bakr [is] contrary to it and that he is the true imam, and none of the mentioned attributes are necessary for him, for being Hashimi is impossible for him, and the other two are not necessary for him by consensus.
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of three conditions proposed by Shi'a groups: being Hashimi, possessing actual knowledge of all religious matters, and performing miracles. The commentator states that these are invalidated by the recognized imamate of Abu Bakr, who lacked all three. Consensus holds that these attributes are not required for an imam.

Key Terms
هاشمي
عالم
معجزة
غلاة
إمامية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe an imam must be Hashimi, know all religious matters actually, and perform miracles.
Correction: Sunni consensus, as argued here, holds that Abu Bakr's imamate proves these conditions are not necessary.

Checkpoint
Which condition is refuted by the recognized imamate of Abu Bakr?
A) Being Qurayshi only.
B) Being Hashimi, knowledgeable, and performing miracles.
C) Being infallible.
D) Being just.

Key Takeaway
The imamate of Abu Bakr, accepted by consensus, demonstrates that the Shi'a conditions of Hashimi lineage, comprehensive knowledge, and miracle-working are not necessary for a valid imam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that Abu Bakr's imamate, accepted by consensus, invalidates conditions that some consider essential. Is there any belief you hold about religious leadership that you have not tested against the consensus of the early community? Will you examine your assumptions today and submit to what the consensus established, even if it contradicts your sectarian inclinations?
?
The passage states that Abu Bakr did not have comprehensive knowledge, yet he was the imam. Do you trust that Allah chose the right leader even without what you might consider necessary qualifications? Are you willing to follow a leader who may not meet your personal standards, if the community has agreed upon him?
?
Sanusi says the extremists (الغلاة) demand miracles from the imam. Have you ever demanded a sign from God before accepting a truth that is already established by evidence? Repent of such demands and accept what the evidence requires.

Refuting the Infallibility Condition for the Imam
Sanusi refutes infallibility condition with two proofs and responses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni refutation of the condition of infallibility for the imam, including the argument from Abu Bakr's imamate and the responses to the two Shi'a proofs.

Core Idea
Infallibility is not a condition for imamate; even Abu Bakr was not infallible.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses the Shi'a and Ismaili stipulation that the imam must be infallible (ma'sum). He first points to the agreed-upon imamate of Abu Bakr, who was not infallible, as a refutation. Then he presents two arguments used by the opponents and their answers. This lesson walks through these refutations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the structure: a claim, a counter-evidence, two proofs, and two responses.
Pay attention to the concise argumentative style.
2
Understand the Abu Bakr argument
The author says the condition is invalidated by the fact that Abu Bakr's imamate is established by consensus while his infallibility is not obligatory. Recognize this as a historical argument.
This is a direct refutation from established Sunni belief.
3
Analyze the first proof and response
The Shi'a argue that the imam must be infallible because he is needed for teaching or to prevent error. The response denies those purposes, stating the imam's role is to repel harm and baseless opinion.
Notice how the responder reframes the purpose of the imam.
4
Analyze the second proof and response
The opponents cite Quran 2:124 ('My covenant does not reach the wrongdoers'). The response redefines 'wrongdoer' as one who commits a sin negating moral uprightness without repentance, not merely a non-infallible person.
This shows the importance of precise definition in theological debate.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider how these refutations defend the Sunni position that the imam does not require infallibility, and why this matters for the legitimacy of the first caliphs.
The example of Abu Bakr is pivotal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامسة : أن يكون معصوماً، شرطها الإمامية والإسماعيلية؛ ويبطله أن أبا بكر لا تجب عصمته اتفاقاً وثبوت إمامته. احتجوا على اشتراط العصمة بوجهين : الأول : إن الحاجة إلى الإمام إما للتعليم أي لتعليم الناس المعارف الإلهية كما ذهب إليه الملاحدة، ولو جاز جهله وعدم عصمته لما صلح لذلك ولم يفد تعليمه اليقين إذ يجوز حينئذ خطأ فيما علم. وإما لجواز الخطأ على غيره في الأحكام كما ذهب إليه الإمامية. فلو جاز الخطأ عليه أيضاً لم يحصل الغرض منه بل احتاج إلى إمام آخر ويتسلسل. الجواب : منع كون الحاجة إليه لأحدهما، بل لما تقدم من دفع الضرر والمظنون. الثاني : قوله تعالى : ﴿ لا ينال عهدي الظالمين ﴾ [البقرة : ١٢٤]، في جواب إبراهيم عليه السلام حين طلب الإمامة لذريته، وغير المعصوم ظالم فلا يناله عهد الإمامة. الجواب : لا نسلم أن الظالم من ليس بمعصوم بل من ارتكب معصية مسقطة للعدالة مع عدم التوبة والإصلاح.
The fifth: that he be infallible; the Imamiyya and Ismailiyya stipulate it. And it is invalidated by the fact that Abu Bakr's infallibility is not obligatory by agreement while his imamate is established. They argued for stipulating infallibility with two aspects: The first: The need for the imam is either for teaching, i.e., teaching people divine knowledge, as the Mulahida held, and if his ignorance and lack of infallibility were possible, he would not be suitable for that, and his teaching would not yield certainty, for then error would be possible in what he taught. Or it is for the permissibility of error in others regarding rulings, as the Imamiyya held. So if error were also possible for him, the purpose would not be achieved, rather another imam would be needed and there would be an infinite regress. The answer: We deny that the need is for either of these two purposes; rather it is for what preceded: repelling harm and baseless opinion. The second: His saying, exalted be He: 'My covenant does not reach the wrongdoers' (Quran 2:124), in response to Abraham, peace be upon him, when he requested imamate for his descendants, and the non-infallible is a wrongdoer, so the covenant of imamate does not reach him. The answer: We do not accept that the wrongdoer is the one who is not infallible; rather he is one who commits a sin that negates moral uprightness without repentance and reform.
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the condition of infallibility (isma). It states that the infallibility condition is invalidated by the agreed-upon imamate of Abu Bakr, who was not infallible. It then presents two Shi'a proofs and their refutations. The first proof argues that an imam is needed for teaching or to prevent error, so he must be infallible. The refutation denies that the purpose is limited to these; rather, the imam is needed to repel harm and wrong opinion. The second proof cites the Quranic verse that wrongdoers (zalimun) cannot receive the covenant of imamate. The refutation defines 'wrongdoer' as one who commits a major sin without repentance, not as someone who is merely non-infallible.

Key Terms
عصمة
إمامية
إسماعيلية
ظالم
عهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'My covenant does not reach the wrongdoers' means that anyone who is not infallible is a wrongdoer and thus cannot be imam.
Correction: The responder clarifies that 'wrongdoer' is defined as one who commits a sin that negates moral uprightness without repentance, not every non-infallible person. A non-infallible person who avoids major sins can still be an imam.

Checkpoint
According to the responder, what is the correct definition of 'wrongdoer' (zalim) in the context of the verse?
A) Anyone who is not infallible.
B) One who commits a sin that negates moral uprightness without repentance.
C) An idolater.
D) Anyone who sins occasionally.

Key Takeaway
Infallibility is not a condition for imamate; the Sunni position is defended by the example of Abu Bakr and by reinterpreting key Quranic verses.

Imamate Established by Bay'a: The Sunni Doctrine and Its Defense
Sanusi defines establishment of imamate by bay'a and refutes four Shi'a objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni position that imamate is established through bay'a of ahl al-hall wa al-aqd as a sign of divine appointment, and how four key Shi'a objections are answered.

Core Idea
Bay'a is not the cause of imamate but a sign revealing God's choice.

Sanusi presents the third goal of his work on imamate: how is the imam actually established? After stating that mere qualification is insufficient, he affirms that imamate is established either by explicit designation (nass) or by the allegiance (bay'a) of the qualified representatives of the community. This directly opposes the Shi'a position that only nass suffices. To defend the Sunni view, he then addresses four major objections, showing that bay'a functions as a revealing sign (alama) rather than a productive cause.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the need for an act
Read the opening lines: mere suitability does not make one imam; an additional act is required. This sets up the problem.
Sanusi emphasizes that imamate is not automatically conferred by qualification.
2
Identify the two methods
Note the two ways imamate is established: nass (explicit designation) and bay'a (allegiance of ahl al-hall wa al-aqd). This is the core claim.
Bay'a is accepted by Sunnis, Mu'tazila, and some Zaydis, rejected by most Shi'a.
3
Understand bay'a as a sign
In the first objection and answer, Sanusi clarifies that bay'a is not a cause but a sign (alama) indicating God's appointment. Compare to qiyas and ijma'.
This is the key theological move: bay'a reveals, not creates.
4
Work through the remaining objections
Read the second, third, and fourth objections and their answers. See how each answer uses the same logic of bay'a as a sign or appeals to necessity.
Pay attention to the analogies of witness/judge and the case of absent imam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الثالث : فيما تثبت به الإمامة. فإن الشخص بمجرد صلاحه للإمامة وجمعه لشرائطها لا يصير إماماً بل لا بد في ذلك من أمر آخر. وإنها تثبت بالنص من الرسول ومن الإمام السابق بالإجماع، وتثبت أيضاً ببيعة أهل الحل والعقد عند أهل السنة والجماعة والمعتزلة والصالحية من الزيدية خلافاً للشيعة أي لأكثرهم، فإنهم قالوا : لا طريق إلا النص. لنا ثبوت إمامة أبي بكر رضي الله عنه بالبيعة كما سيأتي. احتجوا على عدم انعقادها بالبيعة بوجوه : الأول : الإمامة نيابة الله تعالى والرسول، فلا تثبت بقول الغير الذي هو أهل البيعة إذ لو ثبت بقوله لكان الإمام خليفة عنه لا عن الله ورسوله. قلنا : اختيار أهل البيعة للإمام دليل لنيابة الله ورسوله، نصباه علامة لحكمهما بها أي بتلك النيابة كعلامات سائر الأحكام. وتلخيصه أن البيعة ليست عندنا مثبتة للإمامة حتى يتم ما ذكرتم بل هي علامة مظهرة لها كالأقيسة والإجماعات الدالة على الأحكام الشرعية. الثاني : لا تصرف لأهل البيعة في غيرهم، فلا يصير فعلهم واختيارهم حجة على من عداهم يعني أنهم لا يملكون التصرف بأنفسهم في أمور المسلمين، ومن كان كذلك كيف يملك عليهم شخصاً آخر يتصرف فيهم؟ قلنا : لما كان فعلهم وبيعتهم إمارة منصوبة من جهة الله ورسوله دالة على حكمهما بإمامة من يبايعونه يسقط هذا الكلام إذ تصير بيعتهم حينئذ حجة على المسلمين يجب عليهم اتباعها. وأيضاً فينتقض ما ذكرتموه بالشاهد والحاكم إذ يجب اتباعهما لجعل الشارع قولهما دليلاً على حكم الله الذي يجب اتباعه وإن كانا لا تصرف لهما في المشهود عليه والمحكوم عليه. الثالث : أن القضاء وكذا الحسبة أمر جزئي، ولا ينعقد بالبيعة فكيف تنعقد بها الإمامة العظمى العامة لجميع المسلمين كافة. قلنا : لا نسلم عدم انعقاد القضاء أو الحسبة بالبيعة للخلاف فيه، وإن سلم عدم انعقاده بها فذلك عند وجود الإمام لإمكان الرجوع إليه في هذا المهم، وأما عند عدمه فلا بد من القول بانعقاده بالبيعة تحصيلاً للمصالح المنوطة به ودرءاً للمفاسد المتوقعة دونه. الرابع : ثبوت الإمامة بالبيعة يؤدي إلى الفتنة إذ ربما تبايع أقوام على أئمة في بلد واحد أو بلاد متعددة ويدعي كل منهم أن الإمام هو الذي بايعه. ومثل هذا لم يرد .
The third goal: On what establishes imamate. Indeed the person by mere suitability for imamate and possession of its conditions does not become imam; rather another matter is necessary. And it is established by text (nass) from the Messenger and from the previous imam by consensus, and it is also established by the allegiance (bay'a) of the people of loosening and binding (ahl al-hall wa al-aqd) according to Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a, the Mu'tazila, and the Salihiyya of the Zaydiyya, contrary to the Shi'a, i.e., most of them, for they said: There is no way except text. For us is the establishment of the imamate of Abu Bakr, may God be pleased with him, by bay'a as will come. They argued against its validity by bay'a with aspects: First: The imamate is a vicegerency from God and the Messenger, so it is not established by the saying of another, namely the people of bay'a, for if it were established by his saying, the imam would be his deputy, not the deputy of God and His Messenger. We said: The choice of the people of bay'a for the imam is an evidence for the vicegerency of God and His Messenger; they set it up as a sign for their judgment regarding it, i.e., that vicegerency, like the signs for other rulings. And its summary is that bay'a in our view is not what establishes imamate such that what you mentioned would apply; rather it is a revealing sign for it, like analogies (qiyas) and consensus (ijma') that indicate legal rulings. Second: The people of bay'a have no authority over others, so their action and choice would not become a proof over those besides them; meaning they do not have authority themselves over the affairs of Muslims, and someone like that how can he appoint another person over them to have authority? We said: Since their action and bay'a is a sign set up from the side of God and His Messenger indicating Their judgment regarding the imamate of the one they pledge allegiance to, this speech falls away, for then their bay'a becomes a proof over the Muslims that they must follow. Also what you mentioned is refuted by the witness and the judge, for they must be followed because the Lawgiver has made their statement a proof for the judgment of God that must be followed, even though they have no authority over the witnessed and the judged. Third: Indeed adjudication (qada') and likewise the hisba are particular matters, and they are not established by bay'a, so how can the supreme imamate that is general for all Muslims be established by it? We said: We do not accept the non-establishment of adjudication or hisba by bay'a because of disagreement on it. And if it is conceded that it is not established by it, then that is in the presence of an imam because it is possible to refer to him in this important matter. But in his absence, it is necessary to hold that it is established by bay'a to achieve the interests entrusted to it and to repel the evils expected without it. Fourth: The establishment of imamate by bay'a leads to discord (fitna), for perhaps groups will pledge allegiance to imams in one city or multiple cities, and each of them will claim that the imam is the one to whom he pledged allegiance. And the like of this has not occurred.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof. It begins by stating that merit alone does not make one imam; an additional act is needed. Imamate is established either by explicit designation (nass) from the Prophet or predecessor, or by bay'a of the ahl al-hall wa al-aqd. The author asserts that Abu Bakr's imamate was established by bay'a. Then four Shi'a objections are presented and refuted:
1. Bay'a cannot establish a divine vicegerency. Answer: Bay'a is not the cause but a sign (alama) indicating God's choice, just like qiyas and ijma' indicate rulings.
2. The people of bay'a have no authority over others. Answer: Their bay'a is a divinely appointed sign that becomes binding. Moreover, the analogy of witness and judge shows that their word can be authoritative without them having executive power.
3. Since lesser offices like qada' are not established by bay'a, how can the great imamate be? Answer: There is disagreement; even if conceded, it is only in the presence of an imam; otherwise necessity dictates it.
4. Bay'a causes discord with multiple claimants. Answer: This has not actually occurred, implying it is not a valid objection.

Key Terms
بيعة
أهل الحل والعقد
نص
نيابة
علامة
قضاء
حسبة
فتنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Shi'a believe that bay'a can never establish imamate because divine appointment requires explicit textual designation (nass).
Correction: Sunnis hold that bay'a is a divinely instituted sign (alama) that reveals God's choice, analogous to qiyas and ijma' in legal rulings; it does not create authority but indicates it.

Checkpoint
According to the Ahl al-Sunna position in this passage, what is the function of bay'a in establishing imamate?
A) Bay'a is the direct cause of imamate.
B) Bay'a is a sign that reveals God's appointment.
C) Bay'a is irrelevant; only nass matters.
D) Bay'a is valid only if all Muslims participate.

Key Takeaway
Imamate is established not by merit alone but by the community's bay'a, which serves as a sign of divine appointment, and this position is defended through four refutations of Shi'a objections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the leaders you follow—whether in your community, mosque, or wider society—do you see their authority as a sign from God, or merely a result of human selection? How would this perspective change your loyalty and prayers for them?
?
Sanusi argues that bay'a is a sign, not a cause, and that the people of loosening and binding are simply discovering God's will. Have you ever been part of a decision-making process where you felt you were uncovering God's choice rather than creating it? How did that feel, and what can you do to cultivate that attitude more?
?
The fourth objection warns that bay'a could lead to fitna. Think of a time when a leadership decision in your community caused division. Could Sanusi's response—'it has not occurred'—be applied to your situation, or does your experience challenge his dismissal? What does this teach you about trust in divine wisdom versus fear of human error?
